Professor STINEWAY'S success, which has
become so firmly established throughout Australasia,
is based upon the facts xst. That every case treated,
even in its most remote effects, is specially studied,
thus starting aright. 2nd. The Medicines used are
exactly adapted to suit the case, and are compounded
from the purest, freshest, and refined ingredients,
thus promptly producing results without overdosing
or injury.
I seek cases where all else has failed,
Young and middle-aged men and others, who
suffer from Nervous and Physical Debility,
Exhausted Vitality, Premature Decline, etc.,
can recover the same degree of health, strength,
and energy experienced in former years, by
consulting (in person or by letter, which is
free), Professor STINEWAY, who has re-
stored thousands of sufferers to vigorous man-
hood after all other treatment had failed. It
makes no difference what you have taken, or
•who has failed to cure you. Cases that I accept
for treatment are quickly and permanently
cured.
DO ANY OF THE FOLLOWING SYMPTOMS,
OR CLASS OF SYMPTOMS MEET YOUR
DISEASED CONDITION Are you a sufferer
from bad health in any of its many_forms, consequent
on a lingering Nervous, Chronic, or Functional
Disease? Do you feel nervous, debilitated, fretful,,
timid, and lack the power of will and action ?
Are you subject to loss of memory ; have spells of
fainting ; fullness of blood in the head ; feel listless,
moping, unfit for business or pleasure, and subject to
■ fits of melancholy? Are your kidneys, stomach,
urinary organs, liver, or blood in a disordered con-
dition ? Do you suffer from Rheumatism, Neuralgia,
or other aches and pains? Are you timid, nervous,
and forgetful with your mind continually dwelling on
the subject ? Have you lost confidence in yourself,
and energy for business pursuits ? Are you subject
to restless nights, broken sleep, dreams, palpitation
of the heart, bashfulness, confusion of ideas, aversion
to society, dizziness in the head, dimness of sight,
pimples on the face and back, and other desponding
symptoms ? If you are afflicted, why further neglect
your health or future happiness, when by a single con-
sultation (which costs nothing) you can be placed on
the way to health, strength, and vigour.
All correspondence held sacred. A speedy
and perfect life cure is guaranteed in all cases
I undertake, where doubt exists the sufferer is
frankly informed.
Persons living at a distance who desire
treatment without delay can, by forwarding a
full description of their case and £l, receive
by return of post the medicines required.
Do not despair, even though all others have
failed in your case. No matter what your
complaint may be, call or write—l will cure
you. If you are ill, do not delay IMy vast
experience enables me to guarantee a positive
cure in all cases undertaken. CONSUL-

DR. FLETCHER’S
TREATISE ON NERTOUS DISEASES, THEIR CAUSE*
SYMPTOMS, TREATMENT, AND CURE.
rpilE directions contained in this little treatise will be
found of immense benefit to the sufferer; and if these
directions are observed during the early stages of Nervous
or Sexual Debility, No Medicines will he required , as, by
observing the directions given, the patient Can Cure Him-
self.
This admirable little treatise is specially written to
show a method of counteracting the evil effects of indis-
cretions in early life—overwork, irregularity in eating or
drinking, &c. It is the result of much study and long
and successful experience in the treatment of diseases,
and contains a fund of useful information to every suf-
ferer from the following complaints, viz. Nervous
Debility, Dizziness, Specks before the Eyes, Pains in the
Back, Defective Memory, Loss of Will Power, Loss of
Energy, Spermatorrhoea, Bashfulness, Discontentedness,
Constipation, Liver and Kidney Derangements, Atrophy,
Impotence, Impure Blood, Foul Taste in Mouth, Palpita-
tion of Heart, Wakefulness, Despondency, Excessive Ner-
vousness, Inability to Marry, Premature Decay, and other
symptoms experienced by nervous sufferers.
All Should Send for this Little Treatise.
If the complaint is of long standing and requires
medicine, Dr. Fletcher will GUARANTEE TO CURL
ANY CASE OF NERVOUS or SEXUAL DEBILITY
with ONE COURSE OF MEDICINES LASTING FIVE
WEEKS, and will send with medicines A WRITTEN
GUARANTEE to cure same or return the monies paid.
Dr. Fletcher’s treatment is both simple and effective,
and within the reach of the poorest. It is unlike any
i other, and with it he has cured over 25,000 patients, and
invites patients to investigate his statements by verifying
1 the testimonials published in his treatise. The treatise
I will be forwarded on receipt of two stamps to cover
postage.

Western Australia is a much richer colony than is
generally supposed as regards minerals, agriculture and
pastoralism.— Sir Samuel Griffith.
Professor Brown, of Victoria, who made a thorough
examination of the south-western portion of Western'
Australia, states that not the other colonies only, but
even the people of Western Australia evidently do not
know how much there is waiting for development. He
saw thousands of acres in contiguous blocks of fine choco-
late lands, light clay loams, and peaty soils. He thinks
the important features of the district are its grand climate
and regular rainfall.
“ History repeats itself,” and as surely as Sydney and
Melbourne have made lightning-like progress, so will
PERTH,'
the Capital of West Australia.

SEND FOE Dr. PERRYS FAMOUS WORK ON DISEASES OF THE REPRODUCTIVE ORGANS, FREE ON APPLICATION.
consult DR PERRY, M.R.C.S., L.S.A., etc.
(REGISTERED BY THE MEDICAL BOARD ; OF NEW SOUTH WALES),
Late House Surgeon to the. Great Northern Hospital, Caledonian Road, London,
SPECIALIST in the Treatment of Diseases of the
Reproductive Organs and Nervous Complaints
Dr. PERRY was induced to publicly advertise himself as a
Specialist in this branch of the profession in consequence of the number
of persons suffering from diseases of this nature remaining uncured ; and
this is not surprising, when it is remembered, in consequence of the
general dislike of the profession to undertake these cases, the public
health has suffered, because the majority of persons advertising to treat
these diseases, and calling themselves Doctors, Professors, etc.,
Are Neither the One nor the Other,
and exist chiefly because DULY QUALIFIED MEDICAL PRAC-
TITIONERS have not occupied the field.
Patients can, therefore, readily see the advantage of being treated
by a practitioner who has gone through a thorough Medical, Surgical and
Anatomical Training in the best hospitals ; and the Doctor argues that,
although it may be considered contrary to antiquated medical etiquette
to so advertise, yet he is promoting the usefulness and the dignity of
the profession more by searching out and relieving this class of disease,
than by leaving them severely alone to the care of men who, however good
their intentions, have neither the opportunities or advantages of a regular
gedioal training to become acquainted with the best methods of treating
these complex forms of disease.
I!
3Dr-
Will be happy to advise persons suffering from these
diseases, and as, in many cases,
Nothing but the observance of a few simple directions
as to Self-Management
is required to place the patient in a healthy state, all who
are in any way affected should consult him, and thus have
the benefit of his advice and experience.

The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in tne
“ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-

communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.

desire those sending MSS. to distinctly understand that
we arc not responsible for the preservation or transmission

can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week
for a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, ivith whom we do not wish
to interfere.
SATURDAY, AUGUST 30, 1890.
“ Trusts.”
From the days when the stronger savage
assailed the weaker savage and wrested from
him the spoils of a laborious chase, there has
been no change, up to the present hour, in
the spirit which has actuated the relations
between man and man. Methods have been
qualified, but the oppressive and predatory
spirit survives. The solitary ruffian of the
woods learned to associate with himself other
ruffians, and to appreciate the advantages
derived from combined action. Few single
individuals, no matter how superior in
muscular force, in determination of character,
and in cunning, were capable of overcoming
any two men whom they might encounter or
desire to plunder. But a gang of 40
desperadoes, operating in unison and availing
themselves of the advantages of surprise,
were capable of laying under enforced con-
tribution a community or population of many
hundreds. If the spoils gathered by such a
band were equally partitioned, each member
of it would be enabled to enjoy the ravished
property of scores of individuals, a plunder
acquired with comparative immunity from
resistance or retaliation. The hoard which
had cost months of persevering toil, and,
when agriculture began to be practised, some
degree of foresight and patience to accumu-
late, could be snatched and appropriated in
one hour of rapine. But in every group of
human beings, for whatever purpose as-
sembled, there are always a few individuals
who display a superiority of whatever
qualities may be essential, and who succeed
in obtaining a concession from their com-
rades of more than an equal share of what
they might acquire by their united efibrts.
And there has seldom failed to present him-
self one in particular, developing and dis-
playing a capacity superior to all others
whose talents were recognised as essential to
the fullest realisation of the general objects,
and to w horn was conceded a still larger pro-
portion of the common stock.
* * #
By a convention naturally emanating from
such conditions, the peacefully laborious
submitted formally to the exactions of the
actively predatory. It was found more ad-
vantageous to surrender habitually and with
docility a portion of the fruits of industry,
than to have all wrenched from them at un-
certain but recurrent and inevitable intervals,
with the aggravation of ruthless slaughter
and malicious destruction. From such brute
naturalism has proceeded the earliest
organisations, tribes, with their inferior
chieftains and supreme chief, and thence
by progressions which History has fully
displayed, nations with monarchs, aris-
tocracies, and priestly castes. The pervading
instinct has never flickered and never
altered. The sovereign has continued to be
the man who got the biggest share of the
plunder of the people ; the aristocracy, the
lieutenants who secured the next largest
proportion, and so on till the almost indis-
tinguishable line is reached which separated
the plunderer from the plundered. As the
scale of spoliation expanded, concurrently
with the art of organisation, the plundering
class was constantly augmented, with a cor-
responding diminution in the shares of the
participants. This process has now, in most
European communities, reached a stage at
which the whole organism has become un-
sound. The monarch and the aristocracy,
with the civic functionaries who are essential
instruments of their exactions, press so
heavily upon the industrial class in every
European country that for several centuries
a disposition to revolt has been so obvious
that standing armies have been essential to
intimidate and, if necessary, to slaughter.
Armed mercenaries were at first tolerably
reliable to those ends. Their lot could be
rendered apparently, and perhaps really,
preferable to that of the industrials so long
as the practice of abandoning to military
rapine the entire populations of the scenes
of their operations, and of delivering con-
quered towns to sack, prevailed. When
Alva entered the Netherlands, for example,
with nine thousand choice troops, there
marched with the column two thousand
courtesans to keep them in good humour,
and the conquest of a city converted each
soldier who had been grumbling about un-
paid arrears of stipend into the glorious
master of handsful of gold and jewels, and
the Sultan over a bevy of shrieking maids
and groaning matrons.
* * *
But the diffusion of information and en-
lightenment consequent on the discovery of
the art of printing has discredited the savager
methods of war-waging. The mercenary of
to-day is but a wage-slave, like his toiling
brother, and when provision has been made
for the plunder required by the superior
orders, and the horde of civil agents, further
exactions have been impracticable to the
extent which would be required to confer
upon every soldier the luxurious appoint-
ments of a Pretorian guard. Hence violent
methods of extortion have been almost aban-
doned, in favour of the outwardly milder
and not less efficacious system of organised
chicanery and constitutional imposture.
In recent times, force having become dan-
gerous and discredited in all spheres
of predatory activity, has been superseded
by cunning and fraud. The absolute
suppression of that personal representa-
tive of the era of force—the monarch—in
some countries has not seriously alleviated
the condition of the exploited classes. In
the United States of America, where there
exists no monarch, and practically no stand-
ing army, and in the French Republic,
whence monarchy has been expelled, the
material condition of the people is not per-
ceptibly better than in Great Britain or in
Germany in a greater degree than can be
accounted for by the comparative recency of
settlement in the one case, and by the re-
markable fecundity of the. soil and favour-
ableness of the climate in the other. Thus,
even where the “ first robber” of the melo-
dramatic period of history—viz., the Sove-
reign—has been reduced almost to the im-
potent s' ate of (! ian t Pagan, in ‘ ‘ The Pilgrim’s
Progress,” enfeebled and only able to gnash
his teeth in unavailing rancour, fresh ag
gressors beset the highways of human pro-
gress.
# * *
Astor, of New York, draws from the
Republican inhabitants of that city a larger
revenue than the Czar of Russia enjoys for
his personal gratifications. Vanderbilt
derives from his railways a greater income
than the Emperor of Germany is permitted
to appropriate to himself. The Duke of
Westminster exacts more from the small
section of the population of Great Britain
who pay him taxes under the name of rent
than Queen Victoria is enabled to derive
from the entire nation. In the aggregate
the masses of every civilised nation pay
tribute to the new order of capitalist plun-
derers infinitely more than they contribute
to the old order. The latter are under an
obligation to render some services in return
for their exactions. They defray the cost of
the machinery of the State. Much of that
machinery has, beyond doubt, been devised
with the express object of providing sine-
cures for members and dependents of the
plundering class. But some of it would be
requisite under any circumstances. So much
service and return, at any rate, the people
receive from the old order of exploiters.
But the new order make no return
whatever. They pretend, indeed, to dis-
charge the functions of organisers and
superintendents of industry and commerce.
But their services in those capacities would
be fully compensated by a one-thousandth
part of the riches which they extort. Of
late, however, their condition has com-
menced to become somewhat uneasy and
precarious. Competition among themselves
begins to make serious inroads upon their
profits. Till very recently they have
managed to keep going comfortably by the
aid of two devices. By securing to them-
selves the fruits of other men’s native
ingenuity in the form of labour-saving
machinery, and by steadily contracting
their wage-sheets, each has been able to meet
the diminution in the price of the commodi-
ties they deal in. But the latter of these
resources has found its limit. Labour, by
organising, has brought into exercise a
method of checking the downward course of
remuneration. And where Labour, too
desperately disabled to organise, remains
helpless as yet, the wage-rate has touched
the bottom depth of possible reduction.
When, in certain industries, female labour is
obtained for 16 hours a day for four or five
shillings per week of remuneration—the
wretched recipient ekes out the balance
necessary to support life by walking the
streets—it is not conceivable that human
exertion can be bought cheaper. The next
stage would be for women to live by prosti-
tution and work 16 hours daily for no wage,
but in consideration of being exempt from
summary execution.
- # * *
-•'Confronted by this difficulty, capitalists
nowadays have conceived the idea of reverting
to the priinary methods. They are arranging
to combine in gangs, so as to plunder to
more advantage. The notorious “ Boss ”
Tweed adopted this conception a score of
years ago in a crude form, which, by its very
cssenl ial i haracter, aptly illustrated the origin
and derivation of the idea now become a
common commercial resource. He schemed
to make himself and his associated rascals
master of an existent Government. Therein
he would find the machinery of exploitation
already in operation. All that he needed to
do was to work it to his own advantage.
Having succeeded in obtaining control of the
civic government of New York city, he simply
appropriated a considerable part of the
revenues for himself and his familiars. This
was merely assuming the role, and the per-
quisites, of a monarch and an aristocracy.
But as the established laws did not recognise
this usurpation, and Tweed could command
no armed force adequate to enable him to
subvert the laws and revolutionise the civic
constitution, he failed to consolidate his
position. For a season he was King of New
York; but when his subjects revolted,
thereupon he was merely a detected thief.
The laws were stronger than the robber.
# * *
The moral of Tweed’s enterprise and
collapse lias not been thrown away upon the
predatory class. Their schemes to-day are
carefully concocted so as to operate within
the sanction of existing codes, or, at any rate,
so as not to expose the operators to legal
punishments. The ancient laws which res-
trained and scourged forestalled and monopo-
lists have been superseded, repealed, or
otherwise have fallen into desuetude and out
of the Statute-books. Such as remain un-
repealed, among other absolute enactments
are capable of being evaded by the refined
ingenuity of latter-day conspirators. Com-
binations of capitalists to plunder consumers
by artificially raising the price of com-,
modifies, by the old device of creating an
artificial scarcity by withholding stocks, and
the new plan of arranging for a restricted
production, are no longer punishable. Or,
at least, if punishable, the penalties are em-
bodied in old statutes so long inoperative
that the conspirators either believed them to
bo non-existent, to be forgotten, or to be
evasible. Hence, in the United States, so-
called “ Trusts ” multiply day by day. In
Great Britain, ‘ ‘ Corners ” are concerted. The
other day salt was the article it was designed to
use in order to tax the entire population for
the profit of a band of capitalist conspirators.
Yesterday it was chemicals. To-day it is
Labour. A “ Trust ” with a capital of eighty
millions sterling has been announced with
impudent effrontery, the declared object of
which is, not to make Labour buy some
particular commodity dear, but to constrain
Labour to sell the only article of value it
possesses exertion cheap. The result
Would be, of course, identical in either case.
It makes no difference whether Capital com-
pels Labour to give ninepence-worth of
labour for sixpence or sells it sixpence-worth
of salt for ninepence.
* # #
In countries newly settled, such as Aus-
tralia and America, a mode of extortion
much in favour with Capital is to obtain
exclusive control over some natural oppor-
tunity, or general necessity, under pretence
of a benevolent and enterprising anxiety to
be of service to a community. Under this
category are comprised such enterprises as
the provision of tramway-service for the
city and suburbs of Sydney, and the supply
of water to the town of Broken Hill. In
each of these instances public convenience
and accommodation is the pretence, and ex-
ploitation the intention. In the first case the
design of charging higher fares than are de-
manded on the lines as managed by the
State, is not even disguised. That would
afford profit at one end. Reduction in the
number of employes and contraction in the
rate of wage is unquestionably an unavowed
intention. The practice of a similar syndi-
cate on its Melbourne tramway service fur-
nishes unanswerable testimony to that effect.
Thus two distinct sections of the public
would be levied upon : those who use the
trams and those who work them. Each of
these sections would become tax-payers to
the Tram-Syndicate. Now the tram-users
constitute in reality the entire active popula-
tion of the city and suburbs, or very nearly
so. By bringing them under contribution
the tram-monopolists would occupy essen-
tially the same relation to the populace as
used to be held by a baron of the Middle-
ages, who, from his feudal castle, con-
strained an entire community to pay tribute.
* # *
The instance of the Broken Hill Water
Supply is even more flagitious. In this
case the adventurers coolly proposed that
the Legislature, whose prime function it is
to protect the people from oppression and
fraud, shall confer upon a handful of schemers
a monopoly of the very rain from the skies,
which may fall upon an area of two hundred
square miles of the earth’s surface, whether
it falls upon land the property of the State,
upon land the property of individuals, or
upon land the property of the schemers,
This gift of nature is to be, by the people’s
representatives, jn defiance of natural laws
and of natural justice, by a monstrous per-
version of the very essence of legislative
functions, handed over to a Ring, with spe-
cial leave to that Ring to sell what Provi-
deuce freely, but in that district not too
bountifully, gives, at famine prices. No
P r uj e °t is too shameless, no device too scan-
. lous, for capital to initiate and to adopt,
111 pursuance its greedy quest after
801110 “right to lay the people in
general under contribution. But every
. y the comprehension of the people widens
Sj’ as P °t principles, and apprehends their
a Pphc.ation to particulars. Ihe time is not,
we believe, very far remote when proposi-
tions which have plunder for their motive,
Wl | be recognised with unfailing accuracy
anCl promptitude the instant they are
niooted, and when their instigators, their
participants, and especially their hireling-
sponsors in the Legislature will be visited
the swift and severe retribution of
public disgrace and universal contumely,
bhree the public intelligence becomes fully
seized of the baleful tendency and plundering
intention of pseudo benevolent schemes of
such character, it will not be long before
there w *b be engrafted upon the Constitu-
01b fundamental clauses absolutely invali-
dating an y concession of such nature, even
though the Legislature should by negli-
S ence ’ by cajolery, or by corruption, be
g u ilty of giving it the colour of legality.
The Federation of the World.
It is one of the favourite beliefs of humanity
that some day in the distant future—in the dim
old age of the earth—there will dawn a joyous
millennium when liberty and peace will be
universal and the Federation of all mankind
will make man a little more worth preserving,
and the world a little less conspicuous a failure,
than they are to-day. It is hoped that in that
shadowy era which is to come national hatreds
will die out because all nations will be assimi-
lated, and political strife will be brought to an
end because political boundaries will be obli-
terated ; and sectarian animosities will become
extinct, because the thousands of angry sects
which jar and wrangle in all parts of the earth
will join together and live up to some simple
creed and will cease to consign each other to
perdition because they are unable to agree about
the road to Paradise. The Parliament of
nations, of which Tennvson wrote before he
became old and misanthropic and a baron, is to
be a great congress of peace, and even the pre-
judice and hostility which is-born of difference
of language is to be obliterated by the spread of
a universal tongue, so that races will no longer
lie enemies through that prejudice which is born
of isolation and ignorance. And it is claimed
by many enthusiasts that the world is even now
distinctly tending towards this glorious climax
of civilisation, that there are visible signs of a
leaning towards peaceful arbitration in place of
war, and that man, in this enlightened age,
does not long to take his brother’s scalp and
hang it up in his wigwam with quite the same
ferocious longing which pervaded the length and
breadth of his frame in the days of old. The
great desire of humanity, before the period of
commercial intercourse and missionary enter-
prise set in, was to go out and kill somebody,
but this craving for dissension and human brains
is understood to be suffering a gradual process
of elimination ; and through various agencies,
among which cheap calico and the Gospel are
pre-eminent, it will ultimately pass away, and
then the long-dreamt-of brotherhood of man
will be within measurable distance at last,
* * *
The Christian doctrine of peace has been
spreading for nearly 1900 years, and considering
the great end which is before it, it is gratifying
to note that it keeps on advancing. Its pro-
gress, in the early days of the Church, was slow
and precarious, and more than once it seemed
in danger of being wiped out by aggressive
Paganism; but ultimately the arrogant Con-
stantine took it in hand, and proceeded to kill
anybody who declined to accept the faith which
forbade violence, and ever since then its pro-
gress has been rapid. Russia underwent a
process of universal conversion in a single day
at the bidding of the reigning Tsar, and if any-
body declined to accept the peaceful doctrines
laid before him he was probably knouted and
then beheaded—anyhow, ho left no traces in
history. Western Europe accepted the same
grand creed, partly by persuasion, partly by
more or less vigorous compulsion, and has since
done good service in spreading it throughout
the earth. The Maori or the Indian who
declined to pray got involved in political com-
plications and was shot instead, and the scanty
remnant of the tribes have accepted the doctrine
of peace lest something worse should happen to
them; and throughout the greater part of the
globe the theory of human brotherhood is being
carried every day by men who are able to bring
down anybody who objects to be a brother at
1000 yards and upwards. 1 f civilisation can only
stand the strain, it may abolish war at last by
killing everybody who declines to arbitrate,
and smother national hatreds by putting a head
on any race or nation which refuses to love its
neighbour ; and, so far as affection can be im-
planted with a big gun and an explosive shell,
and the tender flow'er of human sympathy be
cultivated with a club, no effort is being spared
to bring about the great day of Universal
Federation,
* * *
Unfortunately, the civilisation which is to
bring this millennium to pass—if there ever is
any millennium—is itself an ephemeral thing.
It always tends downwards towards the chaos


below. The day that a nation begins to pro.
otoss is also generally the first day of its decay
The Roman who conquered the Avorld Avas the
rude barbarim Avith a hut beside the Tiber, and
hi lost his power and his conquests through
being civilised and refined into the gilded
driveller of the Greek Empire, Avho died at last
of knowledge and imbecility and incurable old
a <re. The same process has ■ been repeated a
dozen times until the present day, when the
Russian —the howling barbarian of the first
century transplanted into the nineteenth —is the
bugbear of Europe, and threatens ere long to
tread down the old civilisations of the West,
and start the world afresh. The progressive
nations of that continent have spent all the long
ages of the Christian era in formulating the first
rudiments of a theory to make life worth having,
and the prospects are that they will not last
long enough to make any use of the knowledge
thus acquired. A thousand ages might possibly
suffice to teach the world the advantages of
universal peace and union, if any nation cared
to undertake the task, but long before that
period the teachers would be extinct and the
races which Avere learning the lesson would have
died and carried the rudiments of their new
knowledge Avith them to the grave. An<l, nnve-
over, man has no time to waste on so slow a
method, and it is a shorter process to teach the
uncivilised millions the blessings of tranquillity
and liberty by blowing up any person avlio
won’t be tranquil, and hanging the individual
who desires his own kind of liberty instead of
the sort that is offered to him. And so the
world Avill progress toAvards Federation until
the boiler bursts, and barbarism Avill move on
towards extinction till the barbarian rises and
declines to be civilised to death any longer, and
the grand theory of universal peace and union
will probably wind up in universal Avar and
chaos.
# * *
The era of universal peace Avill never be
brought about by violence, neither does it seem
likely that it Avill ever be brought about in any
other way. What the uncivilised world has
learned of the blessings of union and universal
brotherhood it has been taught by the SAVord ;
and knowledge so acquired does not amount to
much. The chances are that it would never
have been acquired by any qther means, and
yet, having been acquired in the only available
fashion, it is not worth having. The human
capacity for rising to a civilised condition is
painfully limited, and the capacity for falling
from it is practically infinite. The spread of
knowledge and enlightenment has been com-
paratively rapid of late years, because the
apostles of culture and Christianity have de-
veloped an unexpected facility of extirpating
unenlightened races ; there has been no a vide or
general diffusion of culture, but merely a rough
and ready process of butchering those peoples
Avhich the Avorld was in too great a hurry to
improve or elevate. Judging from what little
remains of the Australian blackfellow, the Red
Indian, the Kaffir, the Hottentot, and other
tribes in similar circumstances, civilisation is
not contagious, and if there is ever to be a
millennium these nations need to be killed
off as a preliminary effort. But, unhappily,
civilisation has brought up at last with a shock
against one or tAvo A r ast aggregations of semi-
barbarians Avho are apparently too numerous to
be killed, and too tough to die a natural death,
and there the process has come to an end. The
European lias never learned lioav to civilise any-
body ; his methods have merely been those of
Mr. S queers who walloped any boy who failed
to assume a cheerful aspect; and, therefore, if
a peaceful Federation of man is ever to be
brought about, the Chinaman and the negro and
various other obstructions have got to bo killed
off as a preliminary. And, unhappily, this is a
somewhat large order, and probably long before
it is completed the European himself will have
fallen from his present high estate and Avill also
Avant to lie wiped out as an obstacle to progress
and enlightenment. And, for that matter, the
European is remarkably poor material at the
best out of Avhich to construct a political and
social millennium, and it is probable that even
the most advanced apostle of Federation would
require to be abolished before Federation Avould
be possible.
* * *
Even if the world should realise its dream of
universal peace and concord, it Avill probably
get tired of it in course of time, and revive the
ancient chaos, and start the whole slow process
of ...evolution all over again. If it abolishes
monarchy in all its forms and acquires uni-
versal freedom and equality, the chances are
that in some future era of senility and mental
decay it avil! resurrect the ancient incubus and
commence the weary struggle for liberty afresh.
If it shakes off the tyranny of Capital, and
abolishes poverty and strife, there is no possible
guarantee that all these venerable burdens will
not be replaced upon its shoulders again by its
own act, only to be removed by the long effort
of centuries of misery. Rienzi gaA’c to the
Romans of the middle-ages the freest constitu-
tion Avhich the world up till then had ever
known, and the citizens of the new common-
wealth butchered him to make way for the
restoration of their ancient tyrants, France
sacrificed one republic for the sake of Napoleon
the Conqueror, and another for Napoleon the
Imbecile—the man of Mexico and Sedan. She
Avas cheerfully prepared to sacrifice a third for
Boulanger —the smallest, meanest charlatan of
his age, if that feathered warrior had afforded
her the opportunity. Even progress grows
wearisome at times, and liberty and prosperity
become monotonous. If Lucifer became tired
of Paradise, it is not improbable that the world
may become tired of the millennium, should
that blessing ever come its Avay.
* * *
If any one desires to ask under these circum-
stances Avhat the world is striving, for it can
only be replied that it is striving for anything
it can get, and getting it anyhow it can. It is
striving for the emancipation of labour, for the
destruction of all manner of old-established
monopolies and abuses Avhich have made the
Avorker a slave and a pariah, for the abolition of
Avar and monarchy and aristocracy and land-
lordism—in short, it is striving for the great
millennium, with a perfect consciousness that it
may never come, that it may not be up to
sample when it does arrive, and that in any case
it is not likely to stay. It is only the things
th it are not worth having that are immortal,
and the survival of the fittest is an exploded
superstition. The Athenian lasted only for a
day and the Chinaman goes on for ever. The
philosopher dies out, but of the pawnbroker there
is no end. The Nihilist is hanged, and the Czar
keeps on living. If the Czar were worth pre-
serving he would probably be dead, and if the
Chinaman were anything else than a menace and
a curse he would be extinct. And if the mil-
lennium of human liberty be worth striving for
it is not likely to last long when it comes, for
nothing is indestructible on earth except cor-
ruption. But still it is better to have possessed
liberty and lost it than never to have possessed
it at all it is better for the human race to be
regenerated, with chaos to come after, than to
be a Dead Sea of humanity without a hope or a
future. The old proverb which the worn-out
voluptuary of Jerusalem passed off on an
ignorant world needs to be 1 turned upside
down ; the dead lion is better than the live dog
after all; or if it is isn’t, then it was only a
second-hand lion when it was alive, and crawled
into the realm of zoology by the back door.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Plea for Property.
The trouble in the Australian shipping-trade
has roused the Melbourne Argus to drivel a
good deal in its oavii uncertain, inconsequent
fashion concerning the rights of Capital and the
inherent cussedness of Labour. The unsubstan-
tial ghosts of the Barnacles have pervaded its
columns, and demanded to know where the
country is going to, and the aged organ has
formed itself into a committee of one Avith itself
in the chair to consider the point, and return a
verdict. And, as the result of its labours, it
presents a resolution to the effect that the com-
panies ‘ ‘ have a right to do what they please Avith
their own steamers,” and that any interference
on the part of the employes is an act of out-
rageous presumption which ought to be put
down. We second this motion cordially, and
urge the Argus to go ahead. The companies
have a perfect right to do as they please with
their oavii steamers. They have a right to
unload at all hours of the day or night without
being interfered with, or called upon to pay
0A T ertime, or subjected to any other obscene
annoyance on the part of the common herd—so
long as the steamer unloads itself; but if it
can’t do that, then the question is not one con-
cerning the vessel—which does belong to the
company —but it concerns the men, who cer-
tainly do not, unless they like to. Also,
the companies have a right to pay what
Avages they please on their own steamers,
providing they are paying the Avages to the
steamers ; but when they are paying a human
being avlio is his own property that person has
a perfect right to kick if he doesn’t think he is
getting a sufficient return for his labour. INen
Avhen the trouble reaches such a point that
officers and crews abandon the vessels simul-
taneously, there is no suggestion on the part of
the most rampant trades-unionist that they
should take the steamers along with them. The
oAvners can do as they like with their oavii, and
if they can run their ships with non-union crews
or without crews, they are at liberty to do so.
And if the Labour organisations should succeed
in preventing neAv crews being shipped to re-
place the old ones, they are still looking after
their oavii business in the strictest sense of the
term. The companies are left to do whatever
they think best with the steamers, which are
their exclusive property, and the men take their
own course with regard to their labour, which
is their personal asset, and each party is attend-
ing to its own affairs. The Argus’s ideas date
back to the time when liberty consisted of the
right of every man to wallop his own nigger,
and it has never yet learned that the ownership
of a vessel does not include the ownership of
the hands and brains without which the vessel is
merely a mass of waste material. Yet the unions,
whose action has roused the bile of the super-
annuated paper to such an extraordinary height,
are merely taking the course which the Argus
itself recommends—they are doing what they
please with their own property. And even in
the present deplorable event of a general
paralysis of the shipping industry they-are still
following the counsels of this solemn monitor.
The organ of Capital called on the owners
to lay up their property rather than give in, and
as the men have laid themselves up they are
following this injunction to the letter. But
whatever quarrels arise, or Avhatever angry
passions may be aroused, the right of the com-
panies to do what they please with their own
must be respected, and if any trades-union or
other body is found unlawfully interfering with
this right we trust that body may be suppressed
by all the available force of the law. Also, if
any decayed newspaper or other organ of re-
spectable stupidity is found endeavouring to
suppress the right of the employes to do as they
think best with their own, we ask that the out-
raged law of property should fall upon it
right off.
A Tribune of the People.
If the legislators of New Zealand are inclined to
give that grand old patriot, Sir George Grey,
a fair show Avhile he is alive, instead of erect-
ing a soulless brass obelisk to his memory when
he is dead, they might abstain from talking-out
his Electoral GoA r ernors Bill when it appears on
the paper next session. For tAvo successiA r e
years that measure has had to go to the Avail
because Mr. Henry Smith Fish, the incendiary
paper-hanger, of Dunedin, wanted to jump on
the ear of his enemy, or because the Skinflint
party desired to talk for 40 days and 40 nights
about the necessity of taking a shilling a week
off the Parliamentary charwoman’s income, and
meanwhile Sir George is growing very old and
his Bill bids fair to accompany him to the
grave. He was the only Democratic Governor
who ever struck New Zealand or any other
colony, and during his tempestuous career his
proceedings frequently made the Colonial Office
Avish that it Avas dead. He got into a bad habit
of sympathising Avith Maoris and other loav
characters, and he manifested a sinful disrespect
for old traditions, and treated red-tape Avith
derision, and wasn’t so solid as he ought
to have been on a great number of
sacred things. Also he had a misguided
tendency to set to work and do anything that
wanted to be done, whereas he ought only to
have made a minute indicating that perhaps it
might be as well if some British noodle ap-
pointed a Commission to find out how not to do
it, and then sent the document to London by
gravel tram, and had it stowed away in a
pigeon-hole. He Avas no more fitted to drivel
washily over foundation-stones and tea-fights
than J ulius Caesar was to take in plain sewing,
and he utterly loathed the boot-licking parochial
Mayor who wanted to read him an ungramma-
tical address without, an “ h ” in it. So at last
he briefly hinted that the Colonial Office had
better find someone there who was common
enough and stupid enough to be a Governor,
and he commenced a ucav career as Premier of
Nbav Zealand, until his Avant of reverence for
old tradition broke his Ministry tip. Then he
became Leader of the Opposition, and scared
his followers away by his democratic ten-
dencies and his utter scorn of party tactics
and other small SAvindles, till at last in
his old age lie is a lonely iconoclast Avith
hardly a follower in the House, and
spends his declining years in capsizing Avorn-out
idols, and making himself offensive to everybody
Avho has an axe to grind at the country’s ex-
pense. He has learned by dismal experience
that a nominee. Governor is expected to be
merely a species of gorged mud-turtle slumber-
ing in a swamp, and as the only statesman in
Australasia who can expound, from his own
personal knowledge, the inner flunkeyism and
imbecility that is required of that imported
ruler, his opinions on the subject are at least
entitled to respect. And therefore the poli-
ticians of Ncav Zealand might at least lend a
hearing to his proposal that the colony should in
future elect its own nominal head, and if it is.
necessary for that purpose to sit on Henry
Smith Fish, then let that obtrusive paper-hanger
be sat upon at once. And even if the dreary
Fergus has to be squashed, and Taiavh anga. to
be smothered in the midst of his eloquence, and
if Mitchelson’s muffled monotone has to be
put down by force of arms in order that the

Dr. Cockbnrn’s Collapse.
The CoOKBpRN (S.A.) Ministry died the other
day, partly of internal weakness and partly of
the Progressive Land-Tax. It was a avcll-
meaning organisation and filled with good inten-
tions, and its great mission was to burst up the
vicious system of land monopoly, and make the
absentee, Avho is draining the Avealth and energies
of the colony, do something for his livelihood.
It ne\ r er had vigour enough to pile nearly so
large an impost as it wanted to on the land-
speculators, and foreign syndicates, and all the
great fraternity of earth-swallowers who have
stolen the best of the country's territory and
done nothing in return, except sell out at an
immense profit and carry away the proceeds,
but in a humble, hesitating fashion it resolved
to make them contribute some, infinitesimal
fragment of their plunder to the national
revenue, and it collapsed in a small, apologetic
Avay as the natural consequence. It did not
fall far, for. mentally it was a short, stumpy
concern, and its head Avas very near the dust
all along. If it had only recognised the great-
ness of the principles for Avlneh it was con-
tending, and made a good fight in the democratic
cause, eA 7 en its ultimate failure might have been
the beginning of a great reform, but it fooled
itself aAvay over a lot of insignificant side-issues,
and died miserably in a squabble about the
break of gauge on the border, and the rusty
condition of the Defence Force, and other ques-
tions of like importance. If Napoleon had
been kicked to death by a mule on the morning
of Austerlitz the end could not liaA’e been much
more ignominious. The fate of the Cockburn
Ministry was the same as that of almost every
other Cabinet in Australia which has threatened
to deal fairly with Capital. For some reason
or other the Australian people seem unable to
comprehend that lawyers, squatters, and land-
grabbers, however wildly democratic they may
be in theory, will sooner see the nation in
Gehenna than legislate against their own hoary
monopolies, and it is a cold day when these three
great parties cannot raise a majority to block
any measure that looks like genuine progress
in any of our parliaments. The Cogkburn
Government was doomed from the start, but
the ignominy of its complicated muddlement
was that it allowed itself to drop to pieces over
a small parochial row, instead of dying for the
only principle it possessed that Avas Avorth
dying for. It had a chance to appear before the
country as having fallen in leading a forlorn
hope, and then it fell instead through a pain in
its stomach, or some similar prosaic ailment.
The consequences are very rough on a party
Avhich deserved better fortune. It is one thing
to holler for a leader who came down fight-
ing, and quite another to holler for a man who
merely came down because he Avas born down
and had never got completely up since.
Judge Hood and Divorce.
Victoria’s new Divorce Act is already getting
tangled up and unworkable through the opera-
tion of that mysterious natural law which always
requires that an Australian statute shall never
mean what it says, but shall always arrive by
hidden ways at some concealed signification.
The first object of the Act was'to give relief to
any Avoman whose husband had deserted her for
a certain length of time, and as the husband
avlio deserts his Avifc seldom leaves his address
behind him -especially in those cases when he
lias gone off' Avith somebody else’s Avife and
taken her husband’s furniture—it a\ as provided
that the actual service of a summons might be
dispensed with, and the proceeding be adver-
tised instead in tlic district whore- the. absentee
was last heard of. Then an ingenious and
fathomless person named Judge Hoon got hold
of this simple-looking proviso, and resolved that
there was an unexpressed intention away down
in the abysses of the Act that no person should
be divorced “without his knowledge,” no
matter though he might have left his home and
gone to reside on the source of the Nile, or.in
the wastes of Spitzbergen ; and, therefore, un-
less the plaintiff could prove satisfactorily
that the defendant was almost sure to see
the advertisement announcing that a ease
had been started against him, this mode
of substituted service could not be allowed.
And as no plaintiff can prove that her
vanished husband is almost certain to see
the printed notice unless she actually knows
where he is, and as not one deserted wife in forty
possess this knowledge, else she would follow
him at once, and seeing that if she owned the
required information there would be no need of
substituted service at all and the defendant could
be sued in the usual way, the Divorce Act, so far
as Judge Hood is concerned, has gone to wreck.
About 19 petitioners out of every 20 are dis-
missed with instructions to go forth and roam
this great inhospitable world till they find out
where the old man has gone to, and if he has
married an ignorant female in Timbuctoo, or
is urging the picturesque camel over the ranges
of Cashmere, she is to tramp round till she dis-
covers him so that she can advertise her
intention of getting a divorce in a paper which
is likely to come under his depraved and
rheumy eye. Meanwhile the other Judges
can’t find that smothered signification in the
Act, but Mr. Hood is positive that it is there ;
so apparently the only thing to be done is to
pass a new Act declaring that the old one meant
exactly what it said, instead of meaning what
Judge Hood thought it ought to have intended
to mean in a concealed or surreptitious sort of
way that wasn’t perceptible to the naked eye.
Japan has started business on a new system
with a constitution and an elective Parliament,
and it is proposed that one of the first duties of
this body shall be to “ establish a new basis' of
morality suited to the conditions of the country. ”■
There is a certain amount of solid sense in this
suggestion, for the European code of morals is
such a lamentable misfit that it is of very little
good to anybody. The parson who preaches it
and expounds it and lives upon it, ends by
eloping with a borrowed female to ’Frisco,
where he lectures “to men only.” The Social
Purity Society which enforces it, gets a
cold in its eye through looking, through the
keyhole of some respectable single lady, and
finds itself cast adrift —an atom with an
inflamed optic wandering loosely through an
unsympathetic world. The politician who
makes laws for its encouragement gets impaled
on the spikes of a back fence while trying to
climb over and interview a Cook, and is mys-
teriously connected with the dead infant which
is found in the suburbs. Everybody esteems
the Ten Commandments, and skirmishes round
after somebody elsc’s wife. Everybody values
the moral law, and nobody cares a hang for it.
And, therefore, what is the good of a code of
ethics which everybody admires, and a good
many snuffle about, and nobody attends to? If
the Japanese Parliament can find some “basis
of morality ” which is suited to the conditions'
of the country, it will have made a good start.

Anyone in the confidence of the New South
Wales railway-employees will speedily arrive
at the conclusion that Chief Railway Commis-
sioner Eddy (once a “ working-man " himself) is
most unpopular with his manual-labour subor-
dinates. Nor do the latter appear to have any
special love for Commissioner Oliver. The
complaint, which we instinctively feel to be
true, is that both these gentlemen, however
good may be their intentions, have an unhappy
knack of eternally emphasising the existence of
a big gap between themselves and their under-
lings. The objection is not so much to what Mr.
Eddy says as to the manner in which lie says
it. The bluff Mr. Fehon, although possessed of
at least as much force of character as either of
Ins colleagues, is the only one of the Commis-
sioners who is fit to act as official spokesman to
men who have worked under the conditions
which have obtained and which in the main
must at all risks preserved in this country.
Mr. Oliver has been accustomed to deal with
servile clerical labour, and Mr. Eddy comes to
New South Wales, a land of sturdy and out-
spoken democracy, red-hot from an aristocratic,
low-waged country, where men still doff their
hats to those who buy their labour.
* * *
If Commissioner Eddy means to be a suc-
cess he must not be half so touchy. The State
pays him £3OOO a year, a stipend which should
go far in helping him to endure even the
roughest of criticism and the windiest of
blather. It was the custom of Frederick
the Great to allow throughout his dominions a
freedom of speech most remarkable for such an
era. Seeing one day a large crowd gathered
round a placard posted high on a wall, the
King rode up and found the placard to contain
a most atrocious slander directed against his
own sacred person. “Take that down, ’ said
the King, “and place it where it may be easily
seen by all. I wish people to say what they
like, fori mean to'do what 1 like.” Mr. Eddy,
so far as the New South Wales railways are
concerned, has been given the power of doing
pretty well what he likes, and with that he
should be perfectly satisfied. When a member
of Parliament talks about “ strangling ” Mr.
Eddy, the latter, if he thinks the threat worth
taking notice of, should simply send notice to
the police.

Ur to now no comment has been made, save in
this journal, concerning the motto adopted by
the N.S.W. authorities for the regimental flag
of the N.S.W, Cadet Corps. As every one
knows, there exists an ancient motto, “Pro
rage saepe, pro patria semper ,” which, being
interpreted, runs “Often for the king, always
for fatherland.” It is the latter half of
the motto only which has been selected, all
reference to the king having been carefully left
out. This very pointed omission is a sign of
the times —and a most artful sop to Australian
Republicanism. As a counterpoise, the chief
flag—“ The Queen’s Own” (!) —is the Union Jack
in rich silk, having on either side a massive
gold-embroidered crown, the pike surmounted
by the Royal crest. There are broidered on
this flag no words. None are necessary. The
standard eloquently says for itself “ Pro rege
semper. ”
It is not yet known, except to his friends and
business intimates, that an individual convicted
four and a-half years ago of a big New South
Wales Land Office fraud and sentenced to ten
years’ gaol, has already obtained his freedom.
The reason assigned for his release will possibly
be the familiar one of 44 ill-health. ’ Not so
very long ago another most influential (and
wealthy) person was convicted of having forged
the name of a dead man to a promissory-note
which he endeavoured to palm off upon the
deceased’s executors, and was sent up for seven
years ; but in two or three years after his little
misfortune the eye of the public rejoiced at the
sight of their dear old friend tooling an irre-
proachable buggy along the main streets of
Sydney, with a rakish tilt- in his well-brushed
belltopper and a big cigar in his mouth. The
authorities, it appears, had been persuaded that
gaol was not good for this gentleman’s health.
The moral of all this is quite obvious. When a
convict is to have his sentence remitted, either
in whole or in part, let the fact be gazetted a
month in advance, so that the nasty, suspicious
public may satisfy itself that the real reason
why mercy is extended is the culprit’s ill-health,
and not the fact that he is in some way or other
related to or mixed up with a member of Par-
liament.
“ Bbitons never, never, never shall be slaves.”
Of course not. Yet the cablegrams tell us that
the British Government insists upon forcing
upon thousands of its subjects a religion they
despise by compelling them to pay the salaries
of clergymen in whom they don’t believe, and
whose ministrations they never attend. A late
advice is :—“ Great difficulty is being experi-
enced in collecting the tithes in Denbighshire.
The tithe-collectors have to be assisted by
troops.” Now, boastful Anglicans, did Rome
ever do anything more brutal than this ? The
Church of England, “by law established a
comfortless heathendom to most Welshmen —-
collects its gospel extortions, its unwarranted
loot, at the point of the bayonet; descends to
the highwayman’s method of forcible robbery.
Rob on and burgle awhile in the name of religion!
The doom of the Established Church is sealed,
and it will never have another ‘ 4 Monarch
Defender of the Faith.” Disestablishment will
first occur in Wales and Scotland, and the end
will not then be far off.

The Australasian steamship owners and their
associates have now fairly thrown down the
gauntlet. They have decided to hold no com-
munication with anyone who speaks of 4 4 black-
legs.” 44 Blacklegs” they will term 44 free
labour.” In a word, they will call Black
44 White.” In other respects also there is to be
no mincing of matters. They will employ no
officer connected with the union. They are going
to break the union, and will, at the end of the
strike re-employ no unionist whose place they
have succeeded in filling by a traitor to the
cause of Labour. The Bulletin assumes that
this can only lie done by importing labour to
replace the unionists. The time has therefore
distinctly arrived when federated labour should
make a definite move to make its friends and
enemies in Parliament declare themselves. Let
a resolution be submitted to the New South
Wales Assembly affirming that the Govern-
ment shall instantly bring in a Bill to make
all contracts to labour in New South
Wales, made beyond the seas, null and void;
let the labour-party circularise Parliament to
the effect that every member is expected to be
in his seat to vote on the resolution—and then
let every man who votes against it, or who
refuses to vote, be remorselessly opposed by the
solid labour-vote until his dying day. That is
the test-question of the present hour, and the
best means of bringing the labour question into
the Parliamentary arena. Australian labour
can be subjugated by the importation of foreign
blacklegs just as easily as it could be subjugated
by the importation of horse, foot, and artillery.
Two white lepers were last week found in New
South Wales—one being a State-school boy of
14 years, and the other a young man of 28.
They have been sent to the leper-hospital at
Little Bay (where there are ten other lepers) as
additional testimonies to the blessings of Chow
labour. Now that leprosy has got a fair start
in the mother colony, it may be just as well to
publish for general information a condensed list
of the symptoms of the hideous disease. The
kind of leprosy oftenest seen*in Australia is
characterised by cutaneous tubercles which
appear in different parts of the skin. They are
hard, rough, and numerous, and terminate in
ulcers which penetrate to the bone and cause
the separation of parts of the body, the toes and
fingers' for example, dropping off. These
symptoms are accompanied with a languor in
motion, dullness of the senses, change of the
voice, and general lethargy. There are three
sorts of leprosy—the scaly, the crustaceous, and
the tuberculous, and one of the best ways to
escape leprosy is to grow your own vegetables.
Sirs James Macßain and Matthew Davies
have arranged a farewell feed to Gov. Cabington,
to be given in the “Queen’s Hall,” Melbourne,
on Sept. 30th. Pabkes has consented to play
a leading part in this greasy demonstration of
K.C.M.G.-devotion to the Throne, and the
Baron is expected to mention that Australia is
“extremely lucky” to get a man like Lord
Jersey. After which Hopetoun will pipe-out
a fervent prayer that he will live to earn the
same measure from Victoria that Cabington
has won from New South Wales, and proceed-
ings will terminate in a general outbreak of
back-scratching. The Cabington banquet
might well be accepted as read, and the daily
newspaper-reports issued immediately. Why
should the Baron, who is certain to feel
bilious at the time, have to sit out such a mum-
mery when he would much rather be enjoying
a quiet yawn in private ?
An Australian Labour Federation proposes that
all its Parliamentary representatives shall
occupy seats on the Opposition cross-benches —
in other words, all the Federation’s labour
members are to declare themselves ‘ ‘ agin the
Government.” “If,” remarks the Mackay
(Q.) Standard, “the Federation is as perfectly
organised and as strong as it would like the
public to believe it is, it will return candidates
for every seat, and the country will see the
rather startling innovation of a Parliament com-
posed entirely of Oppositionists. You can’t put
out a Government which has no existence, and
the whole House being solemnly pledged to bo
Opposition cross-benchers, a most bewildering
sort of deadlock would ensue. Political econo-
mists have never, so far as we are aware, anti-
cipated such a position.”
Concerning the Marine strike, originating
in the desire of the steamship officers to
affiliate with the manual-labour and other
trades-unions, the Melbourne Argus, in a
recent leader, brays thus: “The officers
would lower their position by placing them-
selves on an equal position with their sailors,
and the men would consider themselves superior
to their officers, and general insubordination
must ensue.” This sort of offal makes us feel
more and more tired. Identity of interest is
the only perfect guarantee against insubordina-
tion. Rebellion can never be fostered by the
removal of party feelings. Once establish a
common bond of interest between all hands on
board, and the misdeeds of an individual will
be promptly punished by unanimous condemna-
tion. No officer can lower his position by
admitting himself to be simply a man, instead of
arguing that he is a specially-designed product
of creation. The Argus arguments are simply
unworthy of consideration.
Me. John See, M.L.A., chairman of the Aus-
tralian Newspaper Company, has addressed the
S. M. Herald with reference to the public in-
sinuation of a labor-representative that he (Mr.
See) had, as a steamship-owner, used his news-
paper influence against the strike. Mr. See
flatly denies that he has spoken to an editor of
any Sydney newspaper, or any writer connected
therewith, or written, or caused to be written,
one word in connection with the matter in dis-
pute between the steamship-owners and those
on strike. Mr. See must nevertheless per-
ceive that as in the ordina y course of
human nature no man can be an impartial
judge in his own paramount cause, so no news-
paper editor can fail to be influenced or embar-
rassed by the known opinions and interests of
his paper’s financial controllers. The present
writer once felt this very acutely on assuming
the conduct of a paper in North Queensland,
which organ of public opinion, unfortunately
for its literary tone, had fallen into the hands
of two rum-retailers residing in adjacent and
rival townships. In publican M'Lellan’s town,
where the mines were prosperous, drinks were a
shilling each. In publican O’Hoolan’s region,
where the bottoms had fallen out of the claims,
drinks were sold at sixpence—as a counterpoise
to the attractions of the reefs “ beyant.” The
political, religious, and alcoholic policies of the
two proprietors were utterly at variance, for
M‘Lellan was a fine old Ulster Tory, while Mr.
O’Hoolan had brought both his religious and
political opinions from Cork. And when we
found out that M‘Lellan had got plenty of
money behind him and held a coxxtx'olling in-
terest in the North Queensland Reefer and
Miners’ Friend, how did we plump for slx-illing
drinks and the pious and immortal xnemory of
King Billy !
Let the workmen of New South Wales calmly
ponder over the following impudent piece of
brutality, from the Sydney Echo, run by the
colossally rich Fairfax family :
In Australia we seem to have reached the limit where
high wages can be paid and leave a profit. Yet the use
that is made of the money is to form a fund to foment
trouble and disaster. We see the men who are now out on
strike-respectable workmen, but who will never be any-
thing else than labourers in the race for life. The very
ease with which they lend themselves to the purposes of
agitators shows the quality of their mental capacity. But
the world will never become communistic, and there will
ever he the hewers of wood and the drawers of water.
Stern necessity has taught this truth, and it remains to be
taught to each successive generation. Our own country
has already adopted the only amelioration of this condi-
tion in giving education-free and of the best—to every
child, rich and poor alike, which will enable each indivi-
dual to take that place in life to which his mental calibre
entitles him. But for the rest—the manual labourers—
who have not and will not learn of the better things of
life, to them may only be given the means to support life
in comfort. Anything over and beyond that will but be
used for the injury of mankind, as the present state of
things most surely testifies.
Education, even when superadded to high in-
tellectual capacity, cannot place meix on the
same social plane ; equality of opportunity must
also be offered. Whexx withheld, the money-fat
imbecile has the power if xxot to throttle, at all
events to hobble, the gexxixxs. The men who
have the greatest share of the world's good
things have not, as a rule, acquired that posi-
tion through any virtxxe, either physical or in-
tellectxxal, of their own. It is a lie to say that
‘ ‘ education exxables each individual to reach
that place ixx life to which his mental calibre
entitles him” ; old John Fairfax came to Sydney
as a coxxxpositor ; he had brains, certainly,
and education to some extent, and the
opportunity being offered he became owner of the
big daily, which now chiefly represents unearned
increment. Such opportunities arc not often
presented, and it is not too much to say that
were any of the surviving Fairfaxes now placed
in the paternal primary position, granting them
the ability to uphold it, centuries of labour,
could life be granted to them, would not raise
them to the place finally achieved by the founder
of the family prosperity. “There will
ever be the hewers of wood and the drawers
of water.” True, good Echo, but in those
latter days those who hew wood and draw
water will do so not as now because other occu-
pations are closed to them, but because un-
pleasant occupations are handsomely paid. At
present, metaphorically speaking, the reward of
the hewer is chips, of the drawer, water; but
the day cometh when these despised workers will
live in a manner befitting the men whose exis-
tence is a public necessity. The man whose idea
of hard work is the signing of cheques won’t
have such a good time then, however. Anyhow,
the Echo gives its argument away when it
asserts that to ‘ ‘ manual labourers may only be
given the means to support life in comfort. ’
The employer’s idea of what should constitute
“ comfort ” to his workers unfortunately differs
from their idea; therefore discontent, therefore
strikes.

At a Melbourne fancy ball a young fellow
was lately arrested, in the blue-and-silver costume
of “Faust,” for embezzlement, and locked up
without changing his clothes, being also placed in
the public dock at the police-court wearing the
“ Faust ” attire. Sheer cruelty, this.
When the London Society of Authors gave
a dinner the other night, George Augustus Sala
and his wife indignantly walked out because they
considered their place at the table not important
enough. Concerning this matter a London writer
says:—“Sala is almost the last survivor now of
the school of London hack-writers who learned
their manners from Dickens, and people are in the
habit of deferring a good deal to him on that ac-
count.
That accomplished and truly good woman
“ Australie” (Mrs. Henry Heron, daughter of ex-
Judge Sir William Manning) died on Monday
last of congestion of the lungs. Mrs. Heron,
who was a native of Sydney, possessed a most
graceful literary-style, was a universal favourite
in society, and had long and uncomplainingly
borne a weary burden of trouble. Much sym-
pathy is felt for her venerable father in his
affliction.

It is a pity that “ Fedora” wasn’t put on
sooner at Sydney Criterion, for at present it looks
equal to another month. Failing to give early
notice, the Governors of New South Wales and
Tasmania, who went there the other evening, had
a doubled joy in viewing the performance from
the back row of the circles across the bare shoul-
ders and bald pates of Sydney’s hon-ton. The
respective merits of the two leading performances
have been the fruit of much discussion recently.
Admitting that Mr. Titheradge’s is the more
finished, the more coherent performance, it must
not be forgotten that Ipanoff is the easier person-
age to handle, for throughout his conduct is
straightforward, his motives above-board, and
lie is not, until the very close, distracted
by conflicting emotions. Fedora, on the
other hand, has no sooner recovered from
one emotional paroxysm, than she is attacked by
another of different character, and sometimes
becomes the very shuttlecock of several conflicting-
passions at one moment. Throughout, these
transitions are so abrupt, as to lend the character
a spasmodic tone unless in the hands of one to the
manner horn —a kindred spirit, in fact. Of late
Mi s Achuroh has shown a desire to snatch ap-
plause, to the detriment of ensemble, at inoppor-
tune moments. Applause is as the breath of the
player’s nostrils, but when not obtained legiti-
mately it is detrimental to the recipient, who
must eventually become intensely artificial, tricky
and stagey.

At the Matinee performance of “A Doll’s
House” at Sydney Criterion, last Saturday, the
front five rows of stalls, fenced off for the occasion,
were filled to overflowing by members of the pro-
fession, whose hearty and discriminating applause
must have been peculiarly grateful to their actively-
employed fellows. The stalls and circle were
crowded long before the curtain went up, and the
matinde was a distinct success, artistically and
financially.
« » » »
“Hero and Leander,” contrary to expec-
tation, filled Melbourne Princess’ with money and
enthusiasm on Saturday night. The circumstance
of any classical play being first produced before an
Australian audience was in itself remarkable, and
when the risky experiment proved to have been
carried out with such a perfection of detail, in
scenery and effects, as is seldom seen, the success
achieved was honestly deserving of the generous
demonstration which endorsed it. To the artistic
management of Bellew, equally with the efforts of
painters Brunton and Hennings, the glory of
“Hero and Leander” is mainly attributable. Soul
has been lavished upon the setting of this beauti-
ful show, and the episodes that run most danger
of degenerating into the ridiculous are elevated
into tender suggestions of the sublime. No thought
of failure appeared to daunt the spirit of Satur-
day’s performance. Everybody seemed in earnest
and at ease, and the ancient Greeks—-some of
whom, by the way, looked like the Ancient Order
of Druids—conducted themselves with a classic
composure worthy of a great occasion. The fore-
going rhapsody is intended for real jam.
* * *

minutes after 10 p.m. The first act—lively, leggy
and sacred-musical—deals with the dedication or
Mrs. Hero Potter to the service of Venus-

' signal of distress, a hundred palpitating
girls in the dress-circle are prepared for
the worst. His side-stroke weakens, the
breakers splash up into his quivering nostrils,
and flood him with memories of green fields, and
of his deceased mother, and, finally, one broad
billow lands his corpse upon the pebbles at Mrs.
Potter’s feet. The drowning is a realistic bit of
business. On Saturday the fatal wave slipped its
surface, exposing to view the deal board upon
whose bosom Bellew had been boofed along, yet
the audience didn’t smile. The spell of a poetic
situation was upon them, and they knew the
waters wouldn’t dry up so clumsily next time.
Just here the hard, virtuous priest sneaks on with
his virgins and orders Bellew to be engulfed back
into the place from whence he came, whereupon
Mrs. P. leaps violently over a precipice, and the
clinging forms of the lovers are seen to be ascend-
ing from their watery grave as the trouble ends.

Ibis impossible to say immediately whether
“Hero and Leander ” is an elegantly-worded
play, for Mrs. Potter’s delivery of blank verse
leaves little of its meaning upon the mind, while
others chop their speeches into line lengths or for-
get to speak up. Evidently, however, the author
is something more than a mere ponderous com-
piler of dry sentiments. If any or many lines are
bad, as they might perhaps appear in print, no
doubt tney might have been a blank sight verser,
and the writer’s name merits a mention on the
programme in addition to the simple announce-
ment that the play was “specially written for
Mrs. Potter.” “The Merry Wives of Windsor;
was specially written for Queen Elizabeth, yet it
is usual to chip in a reference to plain Bill Shake-
speare, anyway. No matter. Mrs. Potter acts
(as apart from her talking) very nicely the
character of Hero, and Bellew is the ideal Leander
both afloat and ashore. Sass, too, is quite a classic
revelation as the High Priest; a trying Quasimodo
kind of part is well done by Boulton, and the rest
assist a little towards making this novel entertain-
ment good enough to carry the last three weeks of
the Potter-Bellew season to a triumphant finish.
** * *
“The Parvenu,” at the Bijou, promises to
make a deeper impression than any other of B.
and B.’s Sydney successes produced in Melbourne
during the present season. The comedy has a
good democratic motive, and it is noticeable that
sundry disparaging references to “blue blood,”
or, more particularly, to the snobbery associated
with that ancestral myth, are received here with
heartier applause than fell upon the ears of shud-
dering aristocratians in London at the time when
Edward Ledger instituted a new order of stage-
hero. “The Parvenu,” as now seen, is an almost all-
round improvement upon the original Court Theatre
representation. The natural beauties of No Man s
Land have grown and mellowed like Anson’s con-
ception of the central character. Ledger is a
more likeable embodiment of hard cash than he
used to be, more buoyant in his vulgarity, and less
likely to “improve ” himself into the real cad who
ultimately succeeds in getting Tummy’s legs
under his mahogany and dies of a surfeit of greasy
satisfaction. With an exception in disfavour of
Lawrence, who resembles a bilious crow rather
than an inflated bart., the others suit their places
down to the ground. Misses Homer, Emma
Temple, and Battle Browne; Percy Lyndal and
Boueicault (in the part originally played by his
brother-in-law, Clayton) constitute the others, and
can share a fervent blessing amongst them.
#* * *
All last week “Fun on the Bristol”
enabled the management to hang glad tidings,
printed on cardboard, outside the Opera House
entrances. Why this familiar medley of art and
tom my-nonsense should be a periodical boom in
Melbourne is not quite clear. Even Widdy
O’Brien loses something of her eccentric charm
after a few years, and the new variety items are
not of a ‘lofty nature. Sheridan warbles a
ballad in praise of ready money bookmakers,
who, it appears, can bet longer odds than
the totalisator “if they are put to it” which
Heaven grant they may be, as soon as possible.
Three black-faced geniuses likewise drop into
sporting poetry, and unite in a touching descrip-
tion of how “the favourite is laying last,” as
though the srallant popular fancy were a hen.
What is the use of State schools while talented
artists mortify English grammar thus ? However,
the public seem to revel in this balderdash, and it
evidently makes no difference in the long theatrical
run.

Changes are impending at other houses.
“The Lucky Lot” may keep possession of the
Royal for another week, but audiences are thinning-
down, and the anxiety to see two or three circus-
horses effect phlegmatic jumps over a gate is
diminishing like a quart of strawberries before a
delicate girl, when no young men are near. The
Alexandra has already announced “Alone in
London a solid, comprehensible melodrama —
for Saturday, and the Halls, Waxworks,
Oyclorama, &c., are pegging along with more or
less success. The Vic., by the way, has lowered its
prices.
** * *
George Darrell considers that of all his
dramas, “ The Naked Truth ” is most remarkable
for wealth of “ literary merit.” It is in this work
that George has supplied his heroine with at least
one immortal line, the immortality being attri-
butable to the fact that Robert Browning antioi-
pated our only dramatist, and wrote the line first.
“ I was so young : I had no mother : God forgot
me, and I fell ” (G. Darrell and R Browning).
“1 loved him So ” was unfortunately interpolated
into the Australian version, if we remember
rightly. It never occurred to George that a noble-
minded heroine wouldn’t think it necessary to
offer the excuse that she loved “him” so. He
was wrong to go one better than Browning.

“Hero and Leander ” is now advertised
to start a quarter of an hour later than on Satur-

Fashion seemingly objects to commence walking
home with a wrap round its high-toned head
before 10.30, or else Mrs. Potter is afraid of fend-
ing Fashion earlier away for fear it might think it
hadn’t taken out a full five shillings’-worth.

At Melbourne Princess’ it is against regu-
lations for floral tributes to be handed up by the
leader of the orchestra, so the dudes who come
along with bunches of roses for la belle Potter have
to throw them at her fair head from a private box.
We don’t know whether it is contraband of the
statoots to leave pink, perfumed missives for
Bellew with the stage-doorkeeper.
« « #
A comedian, now in Australia, tells us a
story of how, when the Prince of Wales and his
Alexandra sat in the stage-box of a theatre
where he was engaged, the company played only
to that limited portion of the audience who could
not see into the royal box, for royalty engrossed
the attention of all the others. In fact, royalty
played for the benefit of “loyalty,” for did
“ Tummy ” smile or his wife simper, then a broad
grin went round, and when they laughed, gad-
zooks, the house was convulsed. But the crown-
ing stroke of grovel was made when an actress
with nez retrousse came on the stage; after giving
her one look, our future king turned to his fair
spouse and pressed upwards the point of his good
old Guelphic proboscis with his forefinger; five
seconds later, half the house imitated the action.
What a miserable lot of Liliputians we are in this
world !
** * *
George Darrell was recently persuaded to
favour somebody with a two-column interview,
and in the course of half-a-mile of conversation he
explained that he considered “The Naked Truth”
as being the best dramatic effort which he ever
inflicted upon the public without suffering severe
physical damage and battery. But he adds more
regretfully that “ the truth was too plainly told —
the moral too pointedly drawn,” and, therefore,
he at last withdrew this undressed tale from the
public eye. We are glad to have this explanation,
for, as a rule, the chief objection to George’s
dramas is that everybody is killed except the devil
and the scene-shifter, and why the scene-shifter
isn’t killed too we never could ascertain. Also the
villain looks as if he had slid eight miles on his
ear over a rough road ; and the heroine appears as
if she had swallowed a tombstone with a mournful
inscription on it, and the epitaph had got fixed in
her vitals; and the second murderer grits his
teeth monotonously and says that a “ Time Will
Yet Come,” but it never does ; and the other lady
calls upon tl]e fifth or sixth scoundrel to “ avaunt”
and he won’t do it; and the boss ruffian is per-
petually telling an innocent maid in white that at
last she is in his power and the bottom falls out of
his assertion two minutes afterwards. These and
eighteen more are the principal drawbacks which
have attracted our attention, but it seems that
“The Naked Truth” fell from quite a different
cause. It was too naked for the public taste, and
as the audience wanted their truth with its pants
on a disagreement naturally ensued.

The new theatre to he erected next the
Victoria Coffee Palace, in Pitt street, Sydney, is
at length to be proceeded with, the amended
designs having been completed and a contract for

temple will be named “The Garrick.” Why not
“The Brooke?” That wbuld “go on for ever.”
For Heaven’s sake, let us' at last have something
original.

crone extremely flat. “lolanthe, ’ and repetitions
