At Poverty Point.
Dear Bulletin,—l have received a notice cut from
your paper, saying that I have realised my interests in
South Africa for £24,000, and am going to run an opera
in London for a year at my own expense. Will you
kindly flatly contradict both the above stories. Firstly,
I have not parted with one of my interests in South
Africa, as I am too firm a believer in the future of that
country. Secondly, my opera of “ Isidora” has been ac-
cepted for the Avenue Theatre, London, by the manage-
ment, and instead of my paying a penny towards its
production, I simply receive a percentage on the receipts,
and, of course, should the opera prove a failure, it will be
withdrawn in the usual course. Thirdly, lam still send-
ing out companies to South Africa, and am even now
negotiating with a “ star” who is at present in Australia.
Trusting you will-insert this, as the paragraph mentioned
above is calculated to do me harm, inasmuch as your
journal is widely copied by the London papers.—l am,
yours, &c., Luscombb Seaeelle. London, 11th July.
The proposal to import a military Guards’ band from
England to Melbourne has gone bung. No money to spare
for such a “ long-felt want.”
Fanny Liddiard again injured her knee in Adelaide, and
as it was still weak from the New Zealand accident, the
doctors ordered her to return to Melbourne. It was
feared that the limb might have to be amputated.
Charley Warner will be back again in Australia in about
a year’s time.
Gerald Boothby, a young Adelaide author, is—it has
been stated—writing a comedy for George Rignold.
Boothby composed an operetta which was performed with
fair success at Adelaide Government House before the
Hallos.
At Irving’s “Macbeth” readings (10s. a head admission)
in St. James’s HaM (London), he reads from a desk on one
side of the stage, while Ellen Terry, on the other, picks
up the thread whenever Lady Macbeth has to spin off the
plot.
Alexander Arrow-smith, or, as he is known to the pro-
fession and theatre-goers generally, “ Bill Ryan,” the old
melodramatic actor, a chief supporter of Brooke, Ores*
wick, and other big men, and partner at various times
with B. N. Jones and Johnny Hydes, is living at Abbots-
ford (Vic.), where he spends his time growing pumpkins
and peaches of gargantuan dimensions. He may tread
the boards again, however. His wife, Mrs. W. Kyan, is
one of the best “old women” in the colonies to-day. The
Ryans are a theatrical family—three of the children being
members of the profession. One is Teddy Ryan, of Bland
Holt’s Tragedy Co.
Stead writes originally of the Ober-Ammergau Passion
Play, which mightily influenced him. He mentions that
40,000 Jews were crucified, outside Jerusalem, by Titus,
and yet their blood all fell as upon granite. How, then,
did one crucifixion, of an obscure, poor voung man,
shake the world? The Passion Play actors include the
local publicans, waiters, and everybody, St. John is a
well-known waiter, and the tourists called him with
“ Come here, John 1 Glass o’beer.”

Fanny Kemble writes that the vainest woman she ever
knew' w’as Lady Cook. At 90 years old Lady Cook invited
Fanny to admire her lovely sculpturesque shoulders in her
dressing-room, but the maid said, “ La, for shame, mum 1”
and flung an antimacassar over the reliques. This Lady
Cook was a kleptomaniac. She stole a pet guinea-pig
from a poor working man’s door, took it in her carriage,
and sold it to a turnpike man a few miles further on.
A Melbourne musician, who died lately, was pious, but
stern necessity drove him to play in the theatre orchestra
nearly all his life. It recalls the story of a well-known
German opera fiddler, who never raised his eyes to the
stage, and was once introduced to a prima-donna, before
whom he had rasped 10 years, without ever looking in her
face. Perhaps he used to look at her—er —feet. Who
knows ?

Tolstoi says that love is only the result of over-feeding.
There’s some truth in that, but an American says that it
don’t hold good all round, for the lady-skeleton in a dime
Museum has fallen in love with Succi, the fasting man.
According to Galignani, when Jean Richepin was
reading his play, “ La Glaive,” at the Comedie-Francaise,
a gentleman who overlooked his MS. was surprised to see
“ your legs” written at the opening of each act in large
letters. Richepin, who, when reading, crosses his legs,
had made this note to remind himself to change his posi-
tion in time to avoid cramp.

A lately-fledged Melbourne paper asserts that Anson’s
Fitzjohn Hall in “ The Balloon ” is a “ character study ”
of a Collins-street celebrity. It may be a character study
of, but it is certainly not a character study from the
celebrity in question. Anson played the part in Sydney
nearly eight months ago, and, as a matter of fact, the
actor depicts a type, and no especial individuality.
Charles Holly, the clever minstrel comedian, known so
long in connection with “Tommy” Hudson and Tom
Buckley died at Newtown, Sydney, last week of Bright’s
disease, at the age of 43. He came out to the colonies
first in ’(39 with Frank Hussey. He leaves a widow and
two children.

The Marlowe Memorial performance, in London, a few
weeks ago, was contributed to by many professionals, in-
cluding Sara Bernhardt, Justin H. M'Carthy, M.P., being
deputed to escort her from the carriage, with his best
French. He fetched her in, as he thought, all muffled up,
but was surprised that his glib lingo found no response—it
was Letty Lind dressed for a dance.
At the Theatre Francais, Paris, all the laundrying of
the troupe is paid for by the management, for stage
clothes, even to the petticoats, and so on, worn by the
ladies. The management also pays for the purchase of
everyone’s stage-clothes, and supplies the private ward-
robe of actors who have only £l2 a month and under.

CORRESPONDENCE
“Constant Reader” (St. Kilda, Vie.): The
person who employed the dummy can be made to forfeit
the land and also be imprisoned. The correct course
would be to apply to the local Land Board for an enquirj
and this could best be brought about by the dummy sign-
ing a transfer to some third party, who would thus acquire
a locus standi to come in and challenge the right of the
squatter to hold the land. The dummy could then secure
the forfeiture of the land by proving that he was a
dummy and the squatter knew it and employed him as
such. The dummy might thereafter be tried for his
share of the dummyism, and it would not make any
difference whether he was paid for dummying 01
not, he would be liable all the same, although it
might get him a lighter sentence. If the squatter has
paid up the money due on the selection and obtained a
Crown grant for the land, the matter is, strange to say.
even simpler, because the Supreme Court would proceed
by way of Scire Facias to revoke and cancel the grant.
There have been millions of acres of the best land in N.S.
Wales obtained by these dummying practices, and every
single grant issued to the persons who did the dummying
is voidable, and can be revoked ; and it would benefit
N.S. Wales much if some of the big dummying squatters
were made to disgorge their ill-gotten land, acquired in
direct defiance of the law—“B.L.A.”; Thanks....

with a dynamite accident whereby a piece of his
skull was blown away, has had two ounces of his
brain removed at the local hospital. _ When he
recovers he will only be fit to edit a religious paper.
Says a Sydney daily paper

pounds.

in its attempt to annex a widow’s mite. Amen.
At a dinner once Mr. A having said
something particularly clever, his envious friend,
Mr. B , remarked: “Don’t give him credit
for that epigram, Mr. Wilde; it was the wine
that said it.” “The wine?” exclaimed Oscar;
“then, gentlemen, how pleasant it is to reflect
that our epigrams are, perhaps, now ripening for
us on the vine-covered trellis of some sunny slope
in France.”
What are the demands of the labour-unions
compared with the losses inflicted by the steam-
ship owners one upon another through the medium
of free competition? Judging by the absurdly
low fares recently and, from th.e recent
balance-sheet of the Tasmanian Steamship Naviga-
tion Co., the resolute and masterful firm of
Huddart, Barker & Co. have set themselves to
work to destroy absolutely the former firm. _ Mr.
Commissioner Eddy, who is a firm believer in the
principle of the state-ownership of railways, says
that as in England the loss of profits owing to
competition between private railways is over 20
per cent., he hopes to see the railways of Austra-
lasia consolidated, and run by the confederation
on an absolutely non-competitive basis as an
object-lesson to the world. If the State can run
all the Australian railways, it can also run all the
coasting steamships, and, by abolishing competi-
tion, save an enormous amount of wasted capital.

The Strike.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“ The permanent military forces are held in readiness to
quell any disturbances which should assume undue pio-
i ortions. - Sydney Daily Telegraph.”
They have marshalled for the fight
I n their pride of monied might
And that willing serf of Capital, a cringing, char-
tered Press;
And remorselessly they’ve weighed
As their surest, strongest aid
That conception sheerly brutal, stark Starvation's
sternest stress.
What have we against their might?
We have Justice, we have Right,
We have love of home and children, we have trad
of man in man;
We have memories of the woe
W ' rough t in days of long ago
Ere arid since the toilers banded and the battle
first began.
We have Freedom to defend
To the sheer and bitter end,
For the right to band in unison all toilers great
and small ;
For tlie workers, too, we strive
In the whole vast human hive,
For the war is universal and in our defeat they
fall.
What is all tire wealth they own
But our concrete blood and bone,
Made by virtue of monopoly, a store of idlers’
food.
Thus the cancer spreads and thrives,
And its strength and force derives
From the self-same source from which the workers
also are renewed.
Law and Order! Hear them cry
As if they were doomed to die,
Though we simply curb their inroads, though we
scarcely touch the sore,
Though we let the cancer rest
On a tortured nation’s breast!
bet them save their breath to cry with when we
cut it to the core.
Law and Order ! In our hands
Are the twain. What power stands
In Australia that could punish, if we brushed the
la v aside?
If we choose to tear to shreds
Every law that on ua treads,
Can they bring to bear one engine they have not
already tried ?
Law and Order ! At their source
We could centre all our force ;
We could make the laws we bow to. We shall do
so iu the end.
Ear too long the few have reigned ;
When our triumphs are attained
Will they keep our law and order, as to their laws
now we bend ?
To the battle toilers come,
Though we gain no single crumb
We will cut the loaf in earnest in the future by
our vote.
Though this struggle come to nought,
Sterner battles must be fought—
There’s a good time coming when we’ll grasp the
system by the throat.
EDEARD J. DEMPSEY.
Evening.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Silent in the silent Heaven
Gleams the lonely star of even,
.Floating lamplike in the pallor where the day-
gleam stricken dead
Falls into the ocean boundless,
Of the darkness, waveless, boundless,
Dies, and stains the dim horizon with his heart-
blood, sullen red.
Silent through the ocean silent
Drifts one fleecy-white cloud island
Trailing 1 vaporous o’er the embers of the half-
extinguished West;
Drifts o’er twilight peak and column
To the rayless depths and solemn
Of the darkness that is slumber, of the darkness
that is rest.
All is still. The granite ranges
Sigh no more beneath the changes
Of the wind that died with daylight but one little
hour agone,
As they stand their silence keeping
With the mists of darkness creeping
O’er their shaggy locks of forest and their frozen
brows of stone.
And they stand their fronts uplifting
Mid the dimness that is drifting
Per the blurred fantastic outlines of their thunder-
blasted rocks,
Where the sky and earth-line blending
Sweep around in curve unending,
Broken by the ragged masses of the sombre clumps
of box.
Night advances ; ’neath the trailing
Of her robes the faint gleams failing
Leave the wild, deserted gully to its weird aiid
haunted sleep,
While a deepening blackness swallows
All the rocky cliffs and hollows
Vs the stealthy-footed shadows up the slanting
hillside creep.
Silently the star of even
Gleams from out the darkling heaven
O’er the wild Australian forest, o’er the forms
grotesque and rude
Of the eucalypti an giant,
Rarik on rank, grim and defiant,
Stretching forth gaunt arms Briarean in the
awesome solitude.
All is rest. One last ray lingers,
Just to pass its lucent fingers
O’er the sinuous rocky crannies, where the water
drips along
To the pool that slumbers lonely
Round its granite boulders, only
Shadowed by the rugged denseness of one spreading
kurrajong.
It has fled ; the vision failing
Cannot pierce the silent veiling, ’
Palling, dew-like, from the heaven over r ck and
tree and hill,
As Night’s shadowy tresses streaming,
Lulls them to a deeper dreaming,
While she presses oh their eyelids, as a kiss, her—
“ Peace, be still!”
Even so : for we are weary
Of the long, long hours and dreary,
Of the naked desolation, of the loneliness accurst,
Of the never-sated yearnings,
Of the slow fire’s quenchless burnings.
Of the longing that is hunger, of the longing that
is thirst.
There is none to hear us crying,
And the day seems long in dying,
And the crown of thorns is bitter, bound around
our brows unblest;
But our sun shall have his setting,
And our sorrows their forgetting,
And our sins their absolution, when the night-tide
brings us rest.

A NEW-CHUM'S NATURAL HISTORY FOR CHILDREN.
The venerable Rev. Watsford is the doyen
of Melbourne Wesleyan parsons. He was “ given
up” last year, but is round again, hearty, amazingly
braced up with the Tongan news. He went as a
deputation to Tonga a few years back, being
an old chum of King George; but George Rex
gave him the cold shoulder, egged on by that
rascal Shirley. Mr. Watsford thumps a stick
down now, and expatiateth.
Characteristic sentences from the election
speech of Mr. W. L. Williams, a candidate for
die Borough Council of Sebastopol (Vic.)“ He
had done his best for the welfare of the borough
mid had improved it all he could. The Councillors
also had done their best. They had put a good
fence round the police-station, arid now they
wanted a good lock-up.” Mr. Williams has been
returned at the head of the poll. The electors of
Sebastopol evidently know the value of a man who
looks after their requirements.

“Mors Moriturus Saluts."
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
’Mid the mazy meshings of the tangled skein
Of men’s lying legends I have sought m vain
For a path to guide me, o , ■
For a hole to hide me
From the mocking mem’ries of remorse and pain.
Through the Delphic pages of unwritten lore
Wearily I’ve wandered, seeking (as of yore
Through uncounted ages
So have sought earth’s sag s)
Truth; the life eternal, or the “ nevermore.”
Can’st thou rend my fetters, break life’s gall mg
chain.
Free my tortured spirit, give it rest again,
Quell the mad desires,
Quench the living fires
W hose eonsumeless vengeance sears both he. rt an 1
brain ?
While I yet may linger on the misty brink
Of the Lethean river, ere I stoop to drink ;
But one hope I cherish
That the past may perish ;
That for me waits this eternal—dare I think !
Hail! thou tireless reaper, bearing what you may,
Darkness of oblivion is brighter than my day.
With my passing breath,
Bid you welcome “ Death.”
Strike you must; oh, quickly, therefore, strike I
pray !
“BUNYIP.”
M‘Lellan,the dissatisfied “dove of Ararat,”
in disputing the claim of a certain Frenchman to
be the discoverer of Bendigo goldfield, told the
Victorian Assembly that he (the Dove) took the
first parcel of gold—-251b. weight—from Bendigo
to Castlemaine. “If ever the hon. member was
entrusted with 251 b. weight of gold,” remarked
the irrepressible G. D. Carter, “it certainly is a
great historical fact.”

the students of St.
Patrick’s College, Mel-
bourne, last week that
“ in this country blessed
by nature, and by nature’s
God, every boy who is
innocent and educated
has a glorious prospect
opening in his career.”
No doubt. All such boys
may look forward to be-
coming Directors of
Building Societies. But
it is not necessary that
they should be “ edu-
cated.” Innocence is
enough.

besieged Paris, Theirs

consult with Bismarck
about the proposed capitu-
lation. Of course it was
the Frenchman’s duty to
present a cheerful front,
and to seek to convey the
impression that things
were not m so desperate
a condition in Paris as
the besiegers might sup-
pose. On the other hand,
Bismarck was pretty
well satisfied that the
besieged Parisians were
being starved out, yet,
pf course, lie intimated
po such thing in the pres-
ence of M. Thiers. But
after the. conference Bis-
jnarck invited Thiers to
and the French-
man only too gaily ac-
cepted the invitation.
Then it was that the wily
German noticed th at
Thiers ate voraciously of
the vegetables and breads,
rejected the canned and
pickled foods, and par-
took with seeming avidity
of the fresh meat. This
confirmed Bismarck’s suspicions Paris was
starving.
George Ashton, a retired Government
officer, who resided at Williamstown (Vic.), and
who died the other day, was a man of sense. He
dictated his own instructions as to his funeral.
No advertisement; no attendants ; no mourning-
coaches ; no hatbands, scarves, gloves, &c. ! plain
hearse without plumes or glass sides, and drawn
by two horses without trappings ; no refresh-
ments; and as little mourning costume and no
crape to be worn by his wife and daughters. He
left £11,185.
The Escaped Nun, when last heard of,
was in England, pounding-out the yarn she has
told so often to .so many different audiences, and
scooping the money at the door with all her
ancient vim. The old, celestial fire still ,shines in
her eye, and the pile of this poor, trodden-on
martyr now amounts to a trifle over £30,000.
There are not many things in this world that pay
so well as to be a large, heavy, coarse, hoarse female
who fled from a convent under suspicious circum-
stances. If the escaped individual had devoted
her life to art or literature or plain needlework,
the chances are that she might have toiled for 450
years without getting together one-third of her
present humble competence, but being a person
with a soul that rose above details, and a
stehtoinan top-note, and a man-eating eye, and a
somewhat smudgy narrative, and a comprehensive
estimate of human stupidity, she has come out on
top. The toil-worn female who never escaped out
of anywhere, and who consequently ha * to labour
for 15s a week, will have to study these things,
and try to acquire that gorilla-like expression
which leads to affluence.
Another Great Win!
Mrs. Fanny Dent, wife of a gentleman employeu
in ihe Lambton Collieries, kindly allows us to
publish the following facts of her interesting case
and wonderful recovery, which we gather from
her correspondence, and which we shall be glad to
allow anyone to peruse who wishes to do so.
Mrs. Dent had been in a state of extreme
debility for twelve years, frequently quite un-
able to do any work, the lightest kind causing
great fatigue, with severe pains in the limbs, back
and left side. So severe was the pain that she
would have to sit or lie down frequently. Walk-
ing exercise was very trying and she could only
walk very slowly, She had long seen Clements
Tonic advertised, but like many more people her
husband was prejudiced against “ patent medi-
cines,” but (now w r e use her own wools) “I knew
some one who had tried Clements Tonic, and I
decided to get a bottle on the quiet and say
nothing about it if it did me no good. The effect
I thought something wonderful. I felt better
after the first dose, and by the time I had taken
half a bottle T was a different woman. My
husband, friends and neighbours soon noticed the
change in me, and they could not make it out. 1
had to tell the truth that it was Clements Tonic
that was'making me strong, 1 could not keep the
secret, I was only to glad too give the credit to
Clements Tonic. After finishing the bottle,
work was no trouble to me, but rather a pleasure,
and after doing a heavy day’s washing I could go
for a walk instead of hating to go to bed, as
always happened before taking Clements Tonic.
One of my neighbours said to me last week, ‘ Why,
Mrs. Dent, you are doing wonders this week, how
is it you are so strong, now ? She knew I had done
more that week than I had been able to do in 12
YEARS before (she had known me this time for I
am an old resident of this locality, having lived
here for 15 years.) I replied, ‘Yes, I feel a
wonder to myself, so I’ll tell yon the secret, 1
have been taking Clements Tonic, and I cannot
tell you the benefit I have derived from it. You
know what my sufferings have been and now t
have a genuine taste of good health.’ ”
Mrs. Dent wrote us those particulars of her
remarkable cure in March. She wisely, however,
decided to continue the medicine to “clinch the
cure ”as it were. Some time afterwards she wrote
as follows :
Waratah Commonage, N.S.W
Dear Sir, —It is with great pleasure that I pen
these few lines. I have been as you know a great
sufferer from weakness and debility for years. 1
was under several doctors, but they never did any
good. I DESPAIRED OF EVER BEING
BETTER, but as I stated in my last letter I saw
Clements Tonic advertised. I tried a bottle and
its effect was something wonderful. I felt better
almost immediately, and was a new woman after
finishing the first bottle. Before taking Clements
Tonic it was a great trouble even to do a little
house-work, but afterwards my work was a pleasure
and my friends and neighbours were surprised at
the change in me. I cannot say too much in
praise of it, and can only gratefully describe ray
case and strongly advise all who suffer from weak-
ness, backache, pain in the side, ringing noises in
the ears and shortness of breath to take Clements
Tonic. I feel sure it. will do them as much good
as it has done me.
Yours Gratefully,
FANNY DENT.

Kempthorne, Prosser and Co. ; at Hobart and

A hundred and fifty Grenadiers belonging
to the reserves—comparatively old and fat nurse-
maid-mashers and military flunkeys—have been
ordered to Bermuda “ to infuse a spirit of loyalty
into the younger soldiers.’’ They have gone as
missionaries—in fact, teaching humility and the
national anthem to the rising generation.
Stanley was so seriously ill that on the
morning of the wedding, prior to entering the
Abbey, he went into the deanery and rested on
the sofa for nearly half an hour before the cere-
mony. After the service, the bride and bride-
groom, in passing up the Abbey, crossed over tin-
grave of Livingstone. They took away with
them on their honeymoon “ an experienced nurse.”
Stanley is a howling Tory ; his wife is a howling
Radical.
The French realist, Guy de Maupassant,
was Flaubert’s favourite pupil, and for seven years
studied writing at the feet of his master. Each
week Flaubert would give his young disciple a
subject for an essay or a piece of descriptive
writing, and when the work was done it was sub-
mitted to Flaubert. The great novelist would
then criticise and tear the style to pieces, and
frequently, for his pupil’s guidance, rewrite the
whole thing.
While sowing his wild oats, the Marquis
of Hartington one night played three games of
draughts for £1000 a game, and lost all three.
He then cut a pack of cards for £500 a cut, and in
the course of a very few minutes dropped £5000
more. He never lost for an instant his usual non-
chalant air of sleepy indifference. He never can
get over an occasion when he yawned once during
his own speech in Parliament.

out of his invention.
Now that the Mount Morgan gang are out
of power, perhaps justice will be done in the
shameful case of that good and honest old official,
police-magistrate Lukin.
A “friendly suit” has been commenced
over millionaire Ormond’s residue of £140,000
the question being whether the charities are to
get it, or the poor relations.
The editor of a certain Melbourne publica-
tion is credited with the boast that he gets all his
original matter for 30s. per week, At which price
he pays dearly for his literary staff.
This is the centenary year of Father
Mathew, born in 1790. Father Mathew’s exertions
for the temperance cause once involved him so
heavily that he was imprisoned for debt.
One of Alfred Bennett’s papers contains
an account of the Earl of Jersey’s grandmother.
A much more lively and interesting subject would
be the Earl of Jersey’s great-grandmother.
The Marquis of Londonderry (brother of
the nobleman now “ in trouble ” at Sydney over a
watch-pawning case) advertises that he supplies
coals from his Durham eolleries at specified rates.
A Londoner accuses Walter Besaut of
having hypnotized him, and having extracted
from him in that condition the entire story, word
for word, of the novelette “The Doubts of Dives.
Col. Templeton claims £l2OO from the
Premier Permanent Building Society for services
rendered prior to liquidation. His charge is a
guinea-an-hour, and 100 guineas for “strain and
overtime. ”
Mr. Ernest De Castella, son of the dis-
tinguished Australian vigneron, has been killed
by a fall from his horse. The son of a father who
makes such white wines will be joyfully received
in Heaven.
Two bodies were found in the Yarra at
the same time last week. Both of them had been
called Tait—Hugh Tait and William Tait
respectively—but were not related to each other.
What a grim Tait-a-Tait!
The jar between Professor Drummond and
the Conservative Church world, of Melbourne,
was through his calling the Book of Genesis
“ Oriental Imagery,” because it would not work
in with his support of Darwinian Evolution.
The German Emperor has refused to allow
a statue of his father in Berlin. The money sub-
scribed will be diverted towards a fund for erecting
a colossal statue of him on the Worth Battlefield.
Tfee Kaiser seems afraid of his father’s ghost.
T. P. O’Connor has sold out his share in
London Star for £ls,ooo—after 18 months’ work.
■ Ho put no money into the but merely
gave .„,s services, and, after setting it going, left
fits lieutenants to crystalise it into a success.”
Women in St. Louis, U.S., remember
General Grant coming round, in his blue shirt,'
with “ Want a load of wood to-day, missus ?” He
drove a wood-cart, and used himself to-chuek the
chunks of wood over the fence. No man is much
good until he has been stone-broke.
Some Richmond (Melb.) ladies went all
round the local tobacconists’ a few days ago, ask-
ing _ them to suppress the lascivious window-
exhibited photos., and six promised to tone their
show down. Mrs. Harrison Lee, of marriage-
reform fame, was the moving spirit.
Marlborough’s Duke is quickly making
ducks and drakes of the Yankee widow Hammer-
sley’s coin. They find they cannot live on
£40,000 a year, and the ex-widow is off to New
York to realise on £4OO 000 lying idle, her whole
fortune being (or having been) £1,250,000.
The late Duke of Hamilton, also Duke of
Brandon, in England, and Cluitclherault in France,
used; to sign himself “Ham, Chat and Bran.”
Having once thus signed a letter to a firm of trades-
men, he received an answer addressed, “ Messrs.
Ham, Chat and Bran,” and beginning “Gents.”

Oglethorpe, a wealthy man, who died not
long ago in England, was three times converted to
Catholicism and twice to Protestantism, the way
with him being that he saw all the beauties of a
system from the outside, but all the defects from
the inside. How will this diagnosis fit Ike Selby ?
R.C. Archbishop Carr, compared to his
predecessor, is like a Moorhouse to a Goe, and has
been lecturing Melbourne University students at
large, in the Wilson Hall, on Science and Religion
in a sensible way. The Archbishop is a sound
electrician. He won’t sign himself “T. Mel-
bourne,” but “ T. Carr.”
A Victorian named Keeble, who has just
settled.on the Manning River, N.S.W., is the man
who made the golden horse-shoes, and nailed them
on Mr. Cameron’s horse in Beechworth, in the
year 1855, when Cameron was returned as member
for that district. The four shoes weighed one and
a-half pounds of. solid gold.
Parnell usually wears a loosely-fitting cut-
away-coat. He wears it until it is shiny, then he
wears it until it is shabby, then he keeps on wear-
ing it. He has grown very old-looking in the past
few years. The bald spot on his, head has in-
creased perceptibly in size, and the gr-ay hairs in
his beard are growing more and more numerous.
A well-connected young Victorian attor-
ney was, a year or two ago, divorced from his wife,
who was awarded £4 a week alimony. She
now pulls him up for £6O arrears, although she has
married again. The application failed before a
Melbourne judge a few weeks back. The second
husband must have thought he had a “soft
Girandola.”
Dear Bulletin,— Don’t lay too much
stress on the Spiritualism of Chief Secretary
Deakin. The fact is he was in love at the time,
but hasn’t done much of the harbinger of light
business since he married his excellent wife,
daughter of Junor Brown, the spiritualist and
distiller. Deakin’s as level-headed now as Jack
Woods.—D.
Joe Smith’s pretended “ Book of Mor-
mon ” was an old MS. romance, written by an
eccentric named Spaulding. It lay in a printing-
office about 20 years, when it was appropriated by
Rigdon, a compositor, mate of Smith. Joe read
some extracts at a meeting attended by the widow
Spaulding, who at once cried out: “That's my
husband’s novel !”
POLITICAL POINTS
The Argus wants the leaders of the labor-
union taken to gaol for conspiracy.
S.A. Register has taken the Playford
Ministry under its especial protection.
“ Hunger-thieves ” is a Melbourne news-
paper term for men driven by want to seek a nest
in gaol.
Adelaide Single-tax League has elected
Rev. Hugh Gilmour, a Presbyterian parson, as
president.
In a strike, labour always suffers most.
It is fat, not muscular strength, which supports a
starving man.
Should Mayor Burdekin go to England,
B. R. Wise will, it is said, be put up for his vacant
East Sydney seat.
Melbourne Argus has got a new load on
its chest. It speaks of ‘the purse-proud unions
that order strikes.”
“ The lily, Mr. President, didn’t ought to
be varnished.” —Immortal sentiment by a member of
the N. S. W. Hupper ’Ouse.
Melbourne paper; “The employers are
developing a spirit of Loyalty.” Yes, from Regina
downwards to their noble selves.
The Bulletin predicts that Macrossan
will, with the assistance of Henniker Heaton,
engineer through North Queensland separation.
“ Victoria is being turned into nothing
but a farm !” complains the chairman of the Pas-
toralists’Union. What does he want ? A sheep-
walk ?
Socialist Champion, now in Australia,
wants to know how the Single-Tax would benefit
the man who was willing to work and could get no
work to do.
The Australian Natives’ Association is mad
because Henry George says Australia is but
“ superficially loyal.” The A.N.A. are superficial
in everything.
“We want no assistance. We can get on
without members of Parliament, andean do better
without them. We have seen that in the past.”—
Sydney Labour Unions.
Melbournite reads (in big type) that the
artillery are in readiness in Sydney—against the
employers, of course. Why, that is the way it
was in the French Revolution.
Kingston, who declined to take office in
the Playford Ministry, is playing a waiting game
-probably in conjunction with Holder, the first
of the coming men of S.A. politics.
The longest term of office ever enjoyed by
any S.A. Government is three yearf,' 1881 to 1884 —
by' the Bray Ministry. The shortest is nine days,
October 8 to 17, 1801—Waterhouse Ministry.
H. H. Champion, the great labour*
organiser, is in Melbourne. Let us hope that ere
he leaves Australia, he will deliver some Socialistic
lectures to the clerks and other non-unionists.
Let anyone who wishes to understand the
brutal tyranny under which the humbler English
officials groan, read in Lloyd’s Newspaper by
last mail the accounts of the postmen’s strike.
If prohibition becomes law in N.S.W.
(which Saint Toohey forbid), who is to compensate
the Domain temperance orators, deprived at one
fell swoop of their Sunday trade—and their liquor ?
“Ah,” said a man chattingly, the other
day, to a N.S.W. Naval Brigade officer, “I sup-
pose you’ll be calling out the Brigade in a few days
to put down the strikers.” “My dear, sir,” re-
plied the officer, “ two-thirds of the men belong to
the labour-unions.”
There was a method in the madness of
Willis, M.P., when he talked about “ strangling”
Chief Commissioner Eddy. Willis’ new Sunday
paper, Truth, is now pretty solid with the Sydney
labour unions.
The big strikes in Great Britain mostly
come off during the summer months there. Here,
they almost invariably occur during winter.
Anyone following up this contrast should evolve
some curious causes and effects.
The Cockburn Progressive Land-Tax
brought out C. (t. Roberts, chairman of that huge
land-monopoly, the “S.A. Company ” to Adelaide.
The Company is naturally indignant at the recent
attempt to dip into its big banking-account.
V iStuart, M.L.A., proposes a Plebiscite on
the Sunday Public Library opening, Melbourne.
The Bulletin objects. The fanatical opponents
would all roll up, men, women, and children,
while not a tithe of the supporters would poll.
John Burns and Tom Mann wire on be-
half of the London unions that they are ready to
return if necessary the assistance so generously
given by Australia. Thus will the federation of
the world come about. Union of interests,
“ hands all round,” in fact.
There will be a big rush on the N.S.W.
Bankruptcy Court by local M.sP. just before ne.M
election! Piling your “shovel” gains you tin
sympathy of the electors. It also, in these day-
of payment of members, forms your creditors inn.
a committee to secure your return.
St. James’ Budget (Tory) says working-
men will now be frightened from emigrating to
Australia. You may write absolutely anything
in a newspaper. How many working-men emi-
grate to Australia ? What a bar it will be when
Labour is federated internationally ?
“ Her Majesty has gladly given her assent
■ to the measure passed by the Imperial Parliament
granting Constitutional government to West Aus-
tralia,” lies a cablegram. The Imperial Govern-
ment passed the Act so gladly that it has taken
them three years’ wrangling to do it.
Treasurer Holder’s speech in S.A. Parlia-
ment in the recent no-Confideneo debate amply
justifies The prophecy months ago
that he is the coming Premier of S.A. His
defence of the Ministry was one of the most
brilliant speeches heard in the Chamber for years.
When trades-unionism was in its infancy
the Australian daily Press ridiculed it. When it
became large enough to pat on the head it was
patted accordingly. Now the daily Press is afraid
either to caress it or to stroke it the wrong way.
The daily Press is between the Devil and the deep
sea.
John Haynes’ brother, Dr. Haynes, threa-
tens to stand for Gunnedah. “It is considered
that if Dr. Haynes, J.P., can be induced to stand
his return is a certainty, but he has, up to the
present, declined the seat.” You can’t get any-
thing “straighter” than that. Haynes will be
•there if he can get.
In the Commons, recently, Bradlaugh
moved that the landlord’s privilege to obtain
twelve months’ rent before any other creditor is
paid, be reduced to six months. This was op-
posed by Hicks-Beach and that sham democrat,
“Joe” Chamberlain, who not so long ago proposed
to abolish the landlord’s privilege altogether.
A. G. Taylor writes that the present
N.S.W. Ministry must go, “ not necessarily to be
succeeded by the fanatic worshippers of a cross-
grained, inanimate Protectionist idol, but to pave
the way for the ultimate creation of an advanced
Australian party, rationally reformative in their
endeavour to lay the axe to the root of the shams
and swindles of this generation of political vipers.”
Signs of disaffection towards Duncan
Gillies and his gang are evident even in commercial
circles of Melbourne. At the champagne christen-
ing of Craig and Williamson’s new warehouse, last
week, a shining mercantile light flashed scorn upon
the present Ministry, and solemnly announced
that he only proposed their health as a matter of
courtesy. Which was equivalent to an earnest
wish that Gillies and Co. would retire into the
cold shades of opposition and die as soon as posrble.
The names of two rival candidates (M‘Lean
and Maloney) for a very small N.S.W. post-office
billet were the other day placed before Minister
O’Connor, who promptly said, “ Ap’int Maloney."
Henceforth, whenever Dan O’Connor appears in
public he will be greeted with the gag, “Ap’int
Maloney, ” which in its way is quite as good as the
Yankee currency, “And down went M‘Ginty,”
What a lovely election joke—“ Mr. Chairman, I
wish to ask Mr. O’Connor a question: Who
ap’inted Maloney ?”
Jack Woods, M.L.A,, declares that Hin-
doos and Mohammedans are better than Christians.
Quo’ Jack : “ They have a religion, such as it is,
that they really believe in and act up to. They
are not hypocrites, as we are. They will die for
their religion.” Jim Munro : “Won’t Christians
die for their religion?” Jack Woods ; “I never
saw a Christian yet.” Neither did we, Jack,
except once; and he was expelled from several
churches and from society because he held that
Christ was a Socialist, and he was in gaol every
other while on trumped-up charges of vagrancy
and lunacy, because he regularly (when he had
any) gave all his goods to the poor.
Dear Bulletin, —Do you call the follow-
“jonnick?” Thus a London cablegram The
Dock Labourers’ Union is now firmly established,
and its lists full. It has been decided not to
admit any further members at present.” This
seems to me like shutting-out men and forcing
them to remain non-union, and then execrating
and persecuting them as blacklegs for being non-
union. The position is not unknown among the
trades-unions of Australia.—Yours, Car Hen.
[A representative wharf-labourer declares that
they are willing to give a book, free, to any able-
bodied man applying for membership, and six
months’credit to pay up his subscription.—Ed. B.)

We notice with pleasure that the Equitable
Mortgage, Discount, and Deposit Bank, of 4
Mooi’e-street, Sydney, has recently been incor-
porated, and is now opened for business. This
bank has been formed for the purpose of assisting
those who may at any time be in pecuniary difti
culties and wish to avoid paying the excessive in-
terest which is too often charged. The bank has
secured the able services of Mr. Henry A. Birk-
myre, which should be a guarantee that clients will
be fairly dealt with. In these days of extortion, the
above institution should about fill a long-felt
want.*
If in need of a stimulant take Wolfe’s Schnapps.

An Australian vice-regal attache will occupy
a central position in the divorce-court at an early
date.
* * *
Melbourne will shortly be the scene of a
great divorce boom, there being no less than four
Toorak couples anxious for a judge’s order to free
them from the matrimonial syndicate.
* * #
A not uninfluential citizen of Sydney was
recently shocked and grieved by the notice of his
brother’s death, in Melbourne. The brethren had,
at their last meeting, parted on terms of anger,
but now a great wave of fraternal feeling broke
over the sore heart of the survivor, and he in-
structed a certain firm to make enquiries as to
whether his departed relative was married, and if
the possible widow stood in need of assistance. In
due course came the news that the late Mr.
had been married 21 years, and that happily his
widow and four children were very well provided
fox-. For nearly a quarter of a century these two
sons of the same mother had built up their respec-
tive fortunes in adjoining colonies, neither of
them troubling to make one enquiry concerning
the other until the family hatchet was buried in
a Melbourne grave. Moral —Blood is thicker than
water, but not much.
* # *
It is complained that disappointed and
defeated Government candidates are being given
the most profitable N.S.W. Circuit Crown-Prose-
cutorships.
# * *
For clear and unadulterated economy,
Governor Kintore takes the cake, also the whisky.
When the Cockburn Government were no-con-
fidenced Cockburn himself went home to bathe
his fevered head. A reporter was put on his trail,
but Cockburn refused to be drawn with regard to
his communication to the Governoi’. The scribe,
a quiet, gentlemanly fellow, from the Land o’
Cakes, hastened down to Government House and
asked to see His Excellency at 7 p.m, The
gubernatorial flunkey replied that his lordship
was dressing for a concert, and would see the
reporter a few hours later !—at 10 p.m. The
indefatigable scribe kicked his heels in
Government House once more, and was mes-
saged from His Excellency to the effect
that he was at a supper-party, and would
telephone for him when he x'eturned. At mid-
night the reporter, being telephoned for, went
down to Government House, to be ushered into
the Kintorian crib where H.E. stiffly handed him
the document he had been after, and sat smoking
a cigar with a bottle of whisky and one glass
beside him. Kintore calmly and frugally brewed
himself a glass of toddy, and although he had kept a
“ gentleman of the press” humbxxgging about from
7 p.m. till midnight, never even asked if, in Aus-
tralian phrase, “he had a mouth on him.” Pro-
bably if he had known that that scribe was a rigid
teetotaller, he might have risked it.
* # *
Lady Kintore was costumed as “ A Scotch
Winter” at her recent calico ball—which was no
doubt intended as emblematic of the very Scotch
winter Adelaide society has enjoyed this season.
Taken all round, the ball was a fair success.
* * *
When the flood-dams were in course of
construction at Bourke, Dr. Hawkins offered his
services as honorary physician to the men em-
ployed. He was called up one night, or rather
morning, to see a man, and after a quarter-mile
walk found his patient sitting up in bed with
a pipe in his jaws. “Well, my man,” said the
doctor, “you don’t look very bad.” “No, Doc.,”
was the reply; “ I’m just going to take a dose of
Cockle’s pills, and I want to know how many it’is
safe to swallow,” The doctor’s reply has not been
recorded in Heaven.

Spagnoletti : “Well, well, is Reverend
Colonna-Close yet extant—the wonderful Close ?
I remember him reading the burial service over
Marcus Clarke. Appropriately named is Columnar
Colonna, who followed one Towers as guide,
philosopher and friend to the whole Melbourne
press.”

Px’ops, of the Royal, died in the Alfred
Hospital. He was nigh gone when a friend
dropped in, the day there was a race between a
man and a horse. Props could hardly speak, but
made energetic contortions of his face. “Fetch a
clergyman?” said the friend;, “all right.”
“ N-no ! ” says Props, and twisted out ; “ W-whiclx
won, the man or the horse?”
A French lady started in Melbourne as
“Artiste in Confections,” and Tallowfoot went to
negotiate about a large order in pastry, for a Sir
Gorgius Midas Heliogabulus banquet of peacocks’
tongues, or whatever he could spend most money
on ; but he was surprised to find that confections
meant millinery, corsets, and all them things,
whereupon his coarse mind found suitable vent.
* * #
An enthusiastic squatter, at the Melbourne
sheep-breeding banquet, a few days ago, said he
felt inclined to take off his hat when he saw a
flock of sheep. Nothing like leather, eh? 'Even
Joe Thompson feels inclined to chuck his hat in
the air when he sees a flock of nice, pretty little
shorn lambs, with the wind tempered to ’em, so
that they grow on sweetly for the Cup.
* * *
We begin to think there must be some
occult connection between journalism and trams.
How many pressmen have been killed by tram-
ways in Australia during the last five years?

dation” at Adelaide Government House as a

“At Home.” This defect has only been noticed
by the Kintores, for where on earth used previous
occupants of Government House —Lady Fergus-
son, for instance—to stow their guests, some-
times to _ the extent of nearly a thousand!
The fact is that there are in Adelaide certain
people to whom Lady Kintore has taken an
intense dislike, and to invite them means to invite
their friends. This was one reason why Adelaide
people had such a dull season, and now that “at

* * *
People in a certain Sydney suburb are just
now very shy of accepting invitations to birthday-
parties. Invitations went round recently for a
spree of that description, and, after acceptances
had been sent in, a “friend of the family ” waited
upon the prospective guests and stated that it had
been decided to make the hostess a little present,
limited to 15s. for gentlemen, and 7s. Gd. for ladies.
During the evening the hostess was fairly over-
come upon the unexpected presentation to her of a
handsome silver tea-service.
* # *
Mr. John M‘Master, a hard-headed old
Scotchman, is Mayor of Brisbane. He was elected
to that position through a want of unanimity on
the part of the other aldermen—in a word, he filled
a gap caused by jealousy. He is a merchant in
the Valley, sells potatoes, tea, and chaff in a shop
with a not very pretentious front elevation.
Chang, the Chinese giant, could hardly stretch
hims-lf in it without the risk of lifting the shingles
off the roof. Mac elected to hold the customary
mayoral ball on a Friday night, and thereby gra-
tuitously offended a considerable section of the
community, principally the Jews, whose Sabbath
commences on that evening. Now, one of the
principal wards of the city is represented by a
Jew, who, taking in “the situation,” so to speak,
courteously remonstrated with his Worship before
any fixtures were made. But Mac’s foot, having
been put down, was immovable. Strained rela-
tions now exist between Mayor M‘Master and the
local Hebrews.
• # *
The fond father who promises “ young
hopeful ” a sovereign if he brings home a prize at
Christmas, is generally “had.” The papers for a
certain Australian Grammar-school annual ex-
amination are printed by the father of one of the
pupils. Consequently, when a particular pupil
had the golden nope of the usual sovereign held
out to him the other day, he cheerily replied,
“ All right, dad ; that’ll be ten bob for me and
ten bob for young Brownjones.” Brownjones,
senior, is the printer, and his innocent offspring
sells the examination questions beforehand at half
profits. O ! for the happy, guileless days of child-
hood.

At a recent London restaurant dinner —
the hosts being a common councillor and a book-
maker—the fruit was plucked from trees trans-
planted into fancy tubs. £ls per head was the
cost of this disgusting gorge.

who begin a par. with—“ Surely American jour-
nalism is playing it lower down than ever when it
prints such stuff as this ” —and then reprints a
column of the “stuff,” with a view to relieving
the dreary solemnity of their dirty little organs.

The clergy out of work threaten a union
in N.S.W. “with a view of securing that all
supplies to vacant charges be charged at the rate
of £3OO per annum.” Ha, ha! £3OO a year is
just the price of a member of Parliament.
# * *
Did you ever see a woman reading a news-
paper in a ’bus or on a steamboat? We don’t
remember to have done so. It takes them all
their time to watch the other woman, who is
staring at them.

resting social event;—“ At the menagerie, Gran-
tham, on Friday, 4th inst., A. Gorilla, Esq., of

* * *
A Melbourne female has beaten the record
by charging her husband with conniving at a
criminal assault which could never, say the neigh-
hours and police, have been committed upon her.
If married women can spin these yarns for the
sole purpose of incriminating the old man, some
little excuse must be made for the hysterical
darlings whose wild romances are often the result
of their having no husband to get into trouble.
* * *
“At Box Hill (Melb.) Court two labouring
men were prosecuted, one on a charge of travelling
upon an expired ticket, and the other on a charge
of travelling a few miles beyond his destination
without paying excess fare of The Bench
very pertinently asked how these poor men came
to be prosecuted, while some time ago a number
of persons, including clergymen, officials, mer-
chants, &c. were allowed to go scot-free, although
detected in precisely the same acts of fraud or
negligence.” —(Melbourne paper.)
No one will grudge the S.A. National Gal-
lery its recently-acquired big picture, “ Destiny.”
Since the commencement of the world has ever a
woman lived possessing so microscopic a head and
so gigantic a fore-arm ?
* * *
A lady-writer in a Southern weekly says,
in reference to cricket : —“I really think it is not
a fit game for ladies ; it is undoubtedly a dan-
gerous one, and, as I remarked before, some of
the positions are hardly ladylike.” “Leg,” for
instance ?
* * *
The Primrose League women have pre-
sented the Countess of Jersey with a souvenir, on
her departure to spread the gospel among their
large, red-faced sisters in Sydney. This graceful
token, we imagine, took the form of a gold brooch,
bearing a jewelled representation of the British
lion tickling the kangaroo’s nose with Prince of
Wales’ feathers.
Queer occurrence is reported from Arma-
dale (Vic.) A young man was in charge of a bull,
which seemed much attached to him, but he
struck it angrily with a whip and it gored him
badly. Then the bull refused to take food and
died of grief. Then there comes the story of the
Victorian pet pig which, when a child was lost in
the bush, made for it a nest of leaves, as the birds
did for Little Red Riding Hood. Which romantic
stories take one back to the tale of the N.S.W.
mounted constable who, finding on the track a
dead body and beside it a faithful dog, which
growlingly resented his approach, had to shoot
the dog in order that its master might be buried.
On a loud outcry arising as to the inhumanity of
this, the trooper explained that the reason he shot
the dog was that it was eating the corpse. We
ANOTHER RAILWAY JOB.
When some are por
And some are rich,
The blessed lor
Knows which is which.
—Bulletin Proverbs, vol. vii, page SfiS.
On the railway at Melbourne
When a tick. exam, is made,
The wealthy men, or well-born,
Needn’t be a bit afraid
If they happen to have travelled
On the influential nod—
It is nasty to be snavelled,
But they never go to quod.
True, their teeth begin to chatter,
And their noble brow is hot,
Till they find it doesn’t matter
Whether bigwigs pay or not.
If a porter nabs a cleric
Using someone else’s brief,
There is something quite hysteric
In the station-master’s grief.
His conscience it distresses
To demand, in gentle tones,
Their names and their addresses—
Mostly “ Melbourne —Mister Jones,”
The culprits greet ins pleadings
For forgiveness with a wink,
And the end of the proceedings
Is an influential drink.
But when a son of labour
Has the dashed plebeian cheek
To emulate his neighbour,
He is brought before the beak;
The director and the shepherd
Have a license still to rob,
While the working-man is peppered
To the tune of forty bob.
On the railway at Melbourne,
When a tick. exam, comes off,
If a man be rich or well-born,
Or, as “ladies” say, a toff,
He is sure of absolution
From the good old Government,
Which reserves its prosecution
For the bloke without a cent.
AT THE BALL.
Her lip was red, her eye was black,
Her hair was long and flaxen,
The whiteness of her naked back
Was absolutely waxen;
There never lived a girl more chaste
In style or conversation,
Her bodice low exposed the waist
To wordly admiration.
She slew the dudes with haughty glance,
Although one languid scoffer
Avowed he trembled, lest perchance
Her arm-bands might slip off her.
No passion heaved her ample bust,
As pure as snow and colder—
She had not any fleshly lust
Except for showing shoulder.
Vic.
E.F.

Wolfe’s Schnapps should be in every house.
In Bismarck’s youth he fell violently in
love with one Fraulein Johanna von Puttkammer,
whose behind-hand appellation reminds ns strongly
of a Caledonian gathering. Her parents, with,
whom he was acquainted only by letter, stood him
off a while, but at length invited him to visit them,,
no doubt with a view to sampling the young man.
When he rode up to the house all the Puttkammer
clan and many of their "neighbours had assembled
to inspect him outside the house, but the man of
blood-and-iron gave them no time for deliberation,
for though Fraulein von Bultkammer’s parents
nut on an air of grave solemnity, and the young
lady stood with eyes modestly , bent upon the
ground, Bismarck, on alighting, threw his arms
around his sweetheart’s neck and embraced her
vigorously before anybody had time to tell him
that this conduct was hardly the thing. The result
was, however, an immediate betrothal.

* # #
An Australian lady writer asserts that now
that the cut of corsets, by giving women the
appearance of slender-waistedness, has obviated
any necessity for tight-lacing, the fashionable
lady’s struggle is for small hips rather than slim
vVaists. Long contemplation of the undraped,
female human form divine on the glowing can-
vasses of numerous picture-galleries, prompts us
to assert that any such struggle must inevitably
result in disastrous failure. The feminine waist
alone was made for compression.
# * *
An Australian doctor ably characterises
fashionable ladies’ dress as unnaturally dividing
the body into three zones. First the Frigid, on
top, mid nodings on. just where most wanted.
Next the Torrid, under the corset-armour; and
thirdly the Temperate. Three climates carried
about with you, and how do you expect to be
healthy?
* * *
At Brunswick, Vic., Joseph Drinkmilk
was sent up for three months, for lambasting his
wife into insensibility. If Joe Drinkmilk runs
that sort of domestic circus, what would Jim
Swallowhisky do at Brunswick ?

Cable communications corrupt good morals.
* * *
Thus savagely the Launceston (Tas.) Daily
Telegraph
.Sir Robert and Lady Hamilton would not be likely to be
missed even if they left Tasmania for ever, as they take
very fine care to keep themselves and their liberality well
confined to the bosom of their own family. One never
bears of any dances or entertainments at Government
House, except, perhaps, to the “Young Women’s Im-
provement Society,” which held its annual meeting at
Government House some weeks hack. And then, the hill
of fare—. The only regret to Tasmania, I should say,
is that the visit to Lord Carington will have a limit.

Marvellous Melbourne is not to have an
Eiffel Tower after all. We are sorry. It would
have been an appropriate apex to the local
Eiffel-utin.
«- * *
In the Nimes Court in France, a recen
divorce-case came up, in which the wife claimed
as hers certain articles of jewellery presented to
her before their marriage. Prominent among
these was the engagement-ring. The judges de-
cided that not only is a woman entitled, before the
law, to all those articles of value which a lover has
seen fit to present to her before marriage, but that
the “ ring offered for the purpose of recalling the
engagement of two persons, constitutes the mo t
personal and most irrevocable of all souvenirs
given in view of marriage.”
# # *
At the presentation to Dr. Neild, Dr. Le
Fevre aptly remarked that had Neild concentrated
his attention on medical practice he would have
made a large fortune, for everyone knows what
jealous mistresses Physic and the Law are in re-
gard to a man doing outside work. With all his
pains and enormous loss of time over theatrical
criticisms, semi-scientific articles, and so on,
Neild would only get about as much for them
in a week as he could have realised by a few first-
class medical visits, occupying perhaps a couple of
hours, or even less. A professional man simply
slaughters himself by going out of the groove.
The public think that a man cannot handle an
operating-knife or make a diagnosis if lie so far
unbends as to play cricket (in public); and there is
not an old woman in the land who does not firmly
believe that if a doctor visited her attired in a
Panama hat and a white flannel suit, instead of in
a chimney-pot and black frock-coat, she would die
within 48 hours. The doctor has both literally
and metaphorically to “dress his part,” as they
say on the stage, just like the parson. The only
profession in which dress up to now has counted
for nothing is the Press, whose ablest and most
original members have, in our tolerably wide ex-
perience, been utterly indifferent to the voice of
the convenances.
How the Chinaman will Conquer.
A HALF-CASTE’S VIEW.
The Bulletin has received from a half-
Chinese gentleman a long and interesting letter,
from which it makes the following excerpts:
Your leader of 26th July bears the heading
“Will the Chinese overrun the world?” Now,
although your paper is rim on anti-Chinese lines,
aiK | p y oU rs truly, am a half-Chinese, you might
ffk e to' beooW acquainted with my opinions on
the subject if I state them shortly and concisely,
| take no notice of the fact that the usual designa-
tions bestowed on Chinamen in your semi-serious
paragraphs are “leper,” “heathen,” “Chow,”
etc., because, firstly, you write good and sensible
leaders, and, secondly, the abuse any journal may
hurl at the Chinese will affect their future just
about as much as the barking of a dog at the moon
influences a tidal wave. If I have read the article
m question aright, you assume the position to be
this—That the Chinese are bound to mix with the
other races of the earth: will the fusion be the
result of a Chinese conquest or a conquest of the
Chinese, or will it be effected gradually and peace-
fu]]y? My opinion is that the inevitable fusion
w ill be effected partly by a Chinese conquest, and
in a greater degree peacefully and quietly,
# *
The Chinese have been, and are still, at the
bottom, an essentially warlike and conquering
people. The ideas entertained by Europeans 30
or 40 years ago that Chinese were useless as
soldiers have now been dissipated. Read any of
the books bearing on and treating of the Taeping
Rebellion, or the late Franoo-Chinese war, and
you will cease to doubt that Chinese soldiers arc
of stern stuff Gordon thought them and
IT ALL WEIGHS.
OWNER OF HORSE (which has passed thepost first, to jockey) ; “SAY, LAD, STOP
THAT SPITTING THE MUD OUT OF YOUR MOUTH—YOU’LL
WANT SOMETHING TO MAKE UP FOR LOSING YOUR CAP.”
found them to be of first-class quality. And does-
not the fear—ever growing among European
nations that the Chinese will yet enter on a
career of conquest spring from an instinctive
recognition of their infinite military possi-
bilities ? How is it that Europeans never
discuss the probability of the teeming mil-
lions of India conquering the world ? The
answer is that they know the majority of the
natives of India are “ not built that way.” Never-
theless, I do not think that unless goaded by
national insults, or unless, to use M. Hue’s words,
a “Tchengis or a Timour once more appears,”
the Chinese will ever enter on a career of world-
wide conquest. Outlying and adjacent Asiatic
races and countries they will probably rather in-
evitably assimilate, conquer, and annex. The
Malay Archipelago will almost certainly be theirs,
nominally and virtually. Put, further, in a mili-
tary sense, I do not think they will go.
* # *
It is true of the Chinese “ That the
common sense of most holds a fretful realm in
awe.” The Chinese are, in their own way, a well-
educated people, and read extensively. All their
writings and philosophies decry and denounce war
as unnatural and undesirable, as well as mostly
unnecessary. These arguments are specially
applied to wars undertaken for purposes of con-
quest or to acquire military glory. These reason-
ings, operating for centuries, must have, and have
bad.’ considerable influence over an educated
people like the Chinese. Therefore, all Chinese
Governments for centuries past have adopted, in
theory at least and very often in practice, the view
of the national philosopher, who declared that
“ the glory of the most splendid victory is but the
light from a conflagration. ”
* * *
Now, why do I consider that the ultimate
fusion of races will be effected on the whole
peacefully? Because the Chinaman is so (if 1 may
here apply the word) omnivorous in the matter of
women. Place him where you will among the
Indian women of British Columbia, in the
Mauritius, in Africa, in the Sandwich Islands, in
Malaysia—in fact amongst women of any racc, he,
to use a colloquialism, “ tackles them all. _ Of the
readiness of Caucasian women to assimilate or
rather to permanently mate with the Chinaman
hundreds of young fellows like myself are living
and undoubted testimony. All Orientals are
lovers of women, and the Chinaman lias the
additional advantage in that he has no pre-
judices whatsoever. And to the Chinamans
credit, and to that o: his Government, let
this be said : He values his progeny,
no matter from what race or manner of
woman begotten; and to his children, especially
his heirs male, the Chinese Empire opens its arms,
claiming them as her children, and ready and
anxious, if they have any ability whatever, to
enlist them in her service.
» # #
It is no use denying that Caucasian women
arc ready to mix with Chinamen. Although in
the Australias the Chinamen are not of the
higher and more leisured classes of their race, and
suffer under a kind of social ban, European women
I perhaps from the curiosity natural to the sex) in
abundance mix and mate with Chinese. It is the
old form of the forbidden fruit. Depend upon it,
the man who wrote Genesis (this witli all respect)
knew female nature. I have occasionally heard
Chinese who have served in high-class houses and
families unfold tales funnier than their own.
Nature has blessed yours truly with a counten-
ance which might belong to Sitting Bull, a
Spaniard, an Arab or an Italian ice-cream vendor,
and fit any _of , them naturally. In my travels
ladies often enquire of me as to my somewhat
hard.-to-fix-bn nationality. My outspoken reply
that I am a Chinaman never seems to bar me from
their good graces —nay, it seems to act otherwise.
Therefore I say that women of all races being
ready to smile on Chinamen, and that Chinamen
being equally ready to look with favour on all
races of women, together with the number of
Chinese and their peculiarity of being able to live
in any clime, leads me to believe that the ultimate
fusion of races, so far' as the Chinese are con-
cerned, will be more peaceful than, perhaps, may
at first sight be expected. So much for the China-
men.
Our Conquering' Hero.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Shout hooroo for great St. Peter,
Whom the nations love and bless !
Turn your gas on at the meter,
Skiters of the sporting Press !
Let the banners proudly flutter.
Brazen bands create a din —
Bend the knee and rub the butter,
Into Peter’s dusky skin !
Round Ins limbs like lambkins frisk (),
While his toadies glibly tell
How “at home,” and then in ’Frisco,
Peter gave the beggars h ,
Acted like a real good ’un,
Never meeting one defeat;
Heathens have their idols wooden,
Young Australia worships Pete.
Dressed in Piccadilly clobber
He’s a sight for gods and men ;
Fall upon his neck and slobber,
Work him up a rousing “ ben. !”
Never mother had a black son
Greater since Othello died—
Modest in his worth is Jackson —
See, he doesn’t put on “ side !”
Maybe braver men and bigger
Stand aside and shun the light,
But they couldn’t touch theniggei
If they met him in a fight.
Peter’s lively as the kittens,
Fresh as flowrets in the Spring,
When arrayed in boxing-mittens
He cavorts around the ring.
Shout hooroo ! and tell the story.
Story of the bruiser’s fame ;
Wiser people, seeking glory,
Live and die without a name :
Men of learning, arts, and labour,
Peter to the background shoves.
Brother, learn to punch your neighbour
Win distinction with the gloves !
In the IN ew Hebrides 50 years ago there
was not a Christian. The missionaries tackled it,
white men settled in it; now there is neither a
heathen nor a Christian, but a mixed lot of
sectarians.
Varley’s mission amongst the good young
men of Adelaide was not a brilliant success. The
Adelaide papers refused to report his utterances ;
and a large proportion of the local unco guid are
just now in gaol for swindling.
“ Apples from Victoria, New South Wales"
is a headline which appears in large capitals in the
Field. Apparently geographical information is
somewhat scarce in London, which, we gather from
the map, is the principal town of a small island off
the coast of Quid Oireland.
When Maclvor, the 14-flag fire-eater, was
in Australia, he used to rage like a vain heathen
if his name was spelt either Mclvor or MTvor.
He told us once that the full prefix “ Mac,” with
a capital letter after it, indicated the head of the
clan, and that, as he was the head of his clan, his
name was to be spelt “Maclvor.” Now, the
President of the Victorian Legislative Council
spells his name (or the papers spell it for him)
“Macßain.” Can “Scotty the Wrinkler” tell
ns since when has Sir Jimmy been head of the
clan Bain ?
Tlio Victorian police have in hand a com-
plete list of the Ned Kelly family, in,all its rami-
fications, beginning with Red Kelly, Ned s
father, a desperate card, too, in his time, though
not a criminal. The Kelly tribe extends to 70, all
carefully labelled, but all behaving themselves
admirably. Collateral branches are headed by
Wild Wright, &c., and Steve Byrne’s mother, who
once diagnosed the presence of troopers in the
Clenrowan Ranges, merely by seeing-,the sun glint
on an empty sardine-tin, a long way off. This, to
the keen-eyed Mother Byrne, was like Robinson
Crusoe’s footprint on the sand.
Ex-Premier Morehead, of Queensland,
wantonly fooled his office away when he
threatened to increase the whisky-duty, and pile
up the impost on rum and gin. The man who
runs the Parliamentary refreshment-room an-
nounced that the price of drinks would have to go
up, and straightway B. I). Morehead's Govern-
ment melted away like an ice-cream in that re-
vised Sheol which used to be written plain Loll in
other days. If Morehead had beeijy, Moses him-
self with the Ten Commandmcntafmder his arm,
and the seven wise men thrown in, he would pro-
bably have burst just the same. It is safer to
talk back to a policeman, apd have two gastric
complaints, and gout in the Stomach a- well, than
to raise the price of drinks in any free and en-
lightened community.

The U.S. Shipping Subsidy Bills.
“ The two shipping subsidy Bills have passed the
Stmate, and will almost certainly become laws
within the next month. They are bound to have
a marvellous influence upon American shipping.
The Tonnage Bill provides a bounty of 30 cents a
ton for every thousand miles sailed for 10 years,
after which there is to be a reduction of three
cents for the following nine years. Under this
arrangement every American wheat ship leaving
San Francisco for Liverpool, and carrying, say,
2000 tons, will i - eceive from the Government a
gratuity of $5OOO on the round trip. It is
calculated that every sea-going vessel afloat under
the United States flag will receive from $5OOO to
$lO,OOO a year from this time on, and that the
total expenditure for the first year will amount
to $0,000,000, w Inch, of course, will be rapidly in-
creased as time rolls by. Senator Frye, who had
charge of the Bill, said that he had as nuance that
the adoption of the measure would be followed at
once by the organisation of ship-building com-
panies, and in the putting afloat of swift ships to
ply between the United States and every quarter
of the globe. That there will presently be such
activity in the ship building trade as the country
has never seen, may be taken to be an assured
fact. Then there is the steam lino subsidy Bill,
which provides that steamers of the first class, and
capable of being turned into cruisers in time of
war, arts to receive sfi per mile for every
mile steamed. Steamers of a lower class, such
as carry only freight, are to receiv • sub-
sides graduated from $3 to $1 per mile. Postal
mail steamers are all to be of the first class._ This,
of course, saves our Aiutralian line, which the
colonies have so long supported. The subsidies
granted to first-class mail-carriers is the most
generous ever provided by any country. It will
give the Pacific Mail Company’s steamers an im-
mense advantage over those of the Canadian
Pacific Company, and may have the effect of run-
ning them off the Japan and China route. It is
estimated that under this bll $G,O'OO,OOO a
year will at once be called for, with
thv prospect of enlargement as rapidly as
new steamers can bo built. It is plain
that a new era is upon us. The power of money,
■which in these times can aeeompl sh almost any-
thing, is to be liberally used to give us sailing
fleets and steam lines that can afford to plow the
ocean in quest of commerce, until they have found
enough of it to pay them without a subsidy. An
enormous commercial fleet will quickly dispose of
our over-production, and build up an ever expand-
ing trade that will levy contributions from all
nations. With our home market saved by protec-
tion, we shall by these liberal bounties win the
markets of the world."

The Referee.
In face of the general abuse under which the
Australian Eleven at present labours, it is surprising to
find that the Australians have actually scored more runs
than the Britishers in the matches played. Up to July
20th the Australians had played 34 innings for a total of
5275 runs, as against their opponents’ 4725 for 35 innings.
* * #
The Melbourne Athletic Club are anxious to
make a match between Slavin and Jackson, and offer to
lay the Sydney club £1000 to £800, outside the purse, if
the pair meet"in Sydney. Should the men fight in Mel-
bourne, they want the odds the other way about.
* * #
A jockey recently disqualified for 12 months by
the Y.A.T.C. has sent along a strictly comical petition for
remission of sentence. According to his yarn he
“pulled” his mare without compunction, in obedience to
instructions, and was subsequently promised that the first
time she won he would get the whole of the stakes. She
did win in due course, but as the faithful jockey only re-
ceived a bonus of £10, which wasn’t enough to support
his aged mother for a long, weary year, he now desires to
resume active work in the interests of his needy relative.
Any other kind of robber might show as much cause and
reason for being permitted to continue on the job.
» # *
Sir H. Trafford, English millionaire, has laid on
the telephone from his huntsman’s bedroom to the ken-
nels of all the hounds, to quieten ’em when need be or
hear their complaints.
* # *
Hopetoun has hunted at last. He took a wild
dash with the Melbourne hounds from Kew into the wild
woods of Nunawading, which yielded that amazing
character, Fisher the Messiah, who is said to be flourishing
on the more ’ooman principle, though well up in the tooth
by this time. Nine ladies attended the meet. Hopetoun
was in at the death of the fox, but most of the ladies were,
too, so that one argues the run must have been frightfully
severe. Miss Watson got the brush, and Mr. Payne rode a
horse named Pleasure.
The difference between staying power on the
flat and “ across country ” was instanced at Caulfield the
other Saturday. Audacity, a horse notorious for dying
away to nothing at the end of a six-furlong flutter, can-
tered home in a two-mile hurdle race. Circumstances
change the non-stayer into what sporting writers call “a
glutton for distance.” As a matter of fact, any crock can
“stay for ever” if the pace be slow enough to suit it.
“ It’s not the miles we travel, but the pace that kills.”
* « *
It is wired that one Dalton has outrivalled
Webb’s historic Channel swim, but the egg having been
broken, what is the use ? We have always thought this
feat of Webb’s the most remarkable of any kind in the
world’s history, for while in-experts said he might do it,
all the scientific experts said it was blankly impossible.
Such paradoxes are not unfamiliar in other spheres.
* * *
Pile’s gallant old grey, Mahdi, one of the gamest
and most popular of steeplechasers, broke his leg at
Adelaide On Saturday week. The owner, who loved the
nag as though it were his own son, was much cut-up when
the horse was shot, and has had him stuffed. Mahdi
showed-up the Victorian steeplechasers last winter.
* * *
Gov. Kintore, who thinks of embarking in
horseracing, is supposed already to have an interest in
several nags. “Mr. W. A. Horn’s” Tyro came third at a
recent meeting.
* * *
The horse Orient, who won the Wellington
(N.Z.) Steeplechase lately, is 17 years of age, and has run
second and third for years back in steeplechases, and they
do go over the country in Maoriland. Orient’s big weights
kept him back, but this year he got in light on account of
old age, and made them go all the way. Bland Holt’s ex-
stage-horse, Sir Maurice, was second to Orient !

Anagram.—Totalisator (in view of its popu-
larity) : “ O, oil at start.”
* * *
The aboriginal cricketer, “ Bullocky,” who
visited England in 1868 with Charles Lawrence’s abori-
ginal team, died the other day.
* * *
N. York Clipper says that they have three
checker or draught players, in Reed, Barker and Freeman,
who can beat the world. Well, they didn’t beat the
“ Herd Laddie ” much when he was over there. By the
way, In the return match Wyllie heal Mar, the Queens-
land champion.
» * *
On dit that Titan is a vastly-improved animal,
and that good as his style of going was when last in
Sydney, his moving now' is the equine poetry of motion.
His entries at A. J.C. Spring Meeting are confined to the
“ classic ” events, and after all is over, his plunging owner
perhaps won’t be much out of pocket over the 4000-guinea
purchase-money. Carbine is in great “ fix,” and bids fair
to sweep the Spring weighfc-for-age races, as he did the
Autumn. At a glance, Melos appears to be the only horse
likely to prove a thorn in his side, but, then, Mr. Gannon’s
horse appears to be weighted with ill-luck.
# # *
Medina, winner of Sydney Tatt’s. two-year-old
Stakes, is a smart filly, but she lacks size, and judges do
not think she will make her mark as a three-year-old.
There were many in the race merely out for an airing and
waiting for better things to come.
* * *
Bushman—although he did not win—was in
great form in Saturday’s Hurdle-race. He made a
phenomenal leap at the hurdle opposite the stand, and
the writer does not think it could be far behind a record.
* * *
Against Notts, the Australians scored 148 and
115, while their opponents put 145 and 138 together, and
so our men once more went under, to the hardest nut
they’ve had to crack in any year among the county teams
—this time by 20 runs. They defeated Gloucester by
eight wickets, 'scoring 184 and 25 for two wickets, while
the county team made 77 and 130. The total score now
is—Lost, 13 matches ; won, 10; drawn, 8.
* * *
Jockey Moran is one of the straight-goers, and
his mount is usually worth investing a pound upon. On
Tatt’s Saturday he was on the rank outsider Dick, who
won the Spring Handicap.
* # *
An American paper says that the trotter re-
cently purchased by Mr. Foster, of Sydney, was bought
on the condition that he trotted two miles in five minutes
or better. He did the two miles in 4min. 51sec., and if he
hasn’t left his record on the other side of the herring-
pond, should be heard of shortly in Australia.
# * *
Captain Tom Merry, the American Commis-
sioner, who recently visited Australia on the look-out for
brood mares, was interviewed upon his return to the
States. He said he would rather give £3000 for Nordcn-
feldt than £2OOO for any other sire he flaw in Australia.
Next to Nordenfeldt he liked Lochiel. Lochiel has been
purchased by Mr. James Thompson, of Rylstone, for stud
duty, and his new owner will not be displeased when he
hears such an opinion of his horse from so good a judge
of horseflesh.
* * *
At Tattersall’s meeting at Randwick on Satur-
day the geldings more than held their own. Gelding
Paddy won the Tramway Handicap, followed in by two
mares; two Allies and a gelding furnished the placed
animals in the Two-year-old Stakes ; and two geldings ran
first and second for the Spring Handicap. The only entire
to show-up prominently was Pugilist, who won the Selling
Race.

Shades of Jem Ward, Tom Sayers and Company !
Billy Murphy h&s been offered £IOO to show one night at
Broken Hill.
# m *
Hitherto the rule has been “the better the
speaker the worse the fighter,” but Peter Jackson last
week showed that it is possible to raise a man who can talk
as well as box. In replying to the splendid reception given
him at Sydney Athletic Club, Jackson spoke for upwards
of half-an-hour with fluent delivery and well-chosen words,
and as Jackson, previous to his travels, was usually tongue-
tied when compelled to speak before an audience, his
modest, manly and really eloquent address struck his
hearers as hard as one Of his right-handers. Bendigo,
alias Thompson, left the ring for the pulpit, and should
Peter take to the business Pastor Jefleris’ occupation is
gone. Yankeeland is evidently the home of orators.
* * *
A solemn-looking man walked into the office of
j the Lakeport Peavine the other morning, and handed a
paper to the advertising clerk. “ I will pay you top ad-
vertising rates,” he said, “ to have that printed in your
‘ Answers to Correspondents ’ column every week during
the summer.” The item read:—“ Amateur Sailor : The
quickest way to bale out a boat while sailing is to pull out
the plug in the bottom.” “ I’m afraid we can’t do it,”
said the clerk, regretfully, upon which the solemn party
folded up the paper and walked out with a deep sioh.
“ Who is that?” said the editor. “The new coroner.”—
Exchange.

One of the Premier Permanent defendants is
uncle to a particularly well-known Australian boxer.
# * *
Not long ago a London sporting reporter inter-
viewed Spofforth, and, amongst other idiotic questions,
asked if he considered a bowler was “born ” or “ made?”
And the brilliant reply the Demon made was : “ Why,
a fellah has got to be born or how could he be alive to
bowl?”

When the Cooktown (Q.) Naval Brigade
was ordered out to over-awe the strikers, only
sixteen men obeyed. The secretary of the wharf-
labourers, who is a member of the brigade, refused
point-blank.
The Pall Mall Gazette says; ‘ ‘ The
Australian poet, we suppose, is Sladen.” We
have two objections to pelt against that assertion.
Sladen is not an Australian, and Bladen is not a
poet. Parkes is our best poet —judging by the
Pall Mall Gazette’s standard.
When Mr. Shiels told the Victorian
Assembly that hon. members were either fools or
! something else, Dr. L. L. Smith, whose vocation
in life is to ask young men if they feel any giddi-
ness, &c., rose to a point of order, but was sternly
ruled down. “ In the old Assemblies,” muttered
the disgusted apothecary, “no man could have
said that and lived.”
