Millionaire Squatter, to Genteel Shopman;
“Yes, sir, we must fight those fellows.”
Genteel Shopman ; “ Why so ?”
Squatter; “Why, my docile, genteel donkey,
don’t you see, if the shearers and officers have
their way, grass will grow in George and Collins
streets.”
Genteel Shopman : “ Lord ! that would be good
for us ; we might then get a nibble !”
THE PRESS OF LONDON
WHAT IS ITS VERDICT?
While the Press of London is careful not to en-
dorse any advertised remedy or patent invention,
we have all along observed that articles of actual
merit of new invention from which the public are
likely to be benefited are readily taken up and
endorsed; while, on the other hand, advertised
specialities, which do not bear actual value on the
very face, are as eagerly taken up and exposed as
was the case recently when one of the leading
society papers exposed many of the much adver-
tised quack medicines, greatly to the injury
unprincipled proprietors, but of incalculable bene,
fit to the public. When the Press of London com-
bines to throw the full light of investigation on
any subject, the result that invariably follows is,
the good comes to the top, the bad goes straight
to the bottom. As an illustration of what the
London Press thinks of one of the leading adver.
tised articles, we quote from the following : —
Whitehall Review : “ St, Jacobs oil has, it
appears, received no less than six gold medals dur-
ing the past year, for its wonderful efficacy to
‘ Conquer Pain.’ ”
Modern Truth : “We recommend this truly
wonderful remedy—St. Jacobs oil—to everyone
who is suffering from pain. We are personally
acquainted with many marvellous cures effected
by its use. ”
St. Stephen’s Review ; “It is said that a great
number of London postmen would be wholly un-
able to perform their duties were it not for that
famous remedy, St. Jacobs oil, which they apply
to the muscles after a severe day’s work.”
The Salon ; “The remarkable cures effected
by the use of St. Jacobs oil have become the sub-
ject of extensive comment on the part of leading
newspapers.”
Sunday Times (London): “ Prom our personal
experience and inquiries, St. Jacobs oil will ac-
complish all that its proprietors claim for it; its
extraordinary merits are being rapidly recognised,
as shown by the daily increasing demand.”
Weekly Dispatch : “St. Jacobs oil has cured
thousands of cases of rheumatism and neuralgia,
which have resisted treatment for the greater part
of a lifetime.”
Were space to permit of it, the foregoing might
be supplemented to an almost unlimited extent by
extracts from dozens of other leading London
newspapers, but enough has been said to show
that it is not at all to be wondered at that St.
Jacobs oil has a larger sale than any other medi-‘
cine in the world, and, while the proprietors ex-
pend a fortune every year in their advertisements,
it is only actual value in the remedy itself that
will bring out such unqualified and voluntary
endo"sements from so large a number of high-class
papers, some of which are among the most conser-
vative in England, and it is only actual merit in
the remedy itself which will allow the sale of St.
Jacobs oil in England alone to average over 18,000
bottles per week with the demand steadily increas-
ing, while the sale of other remedies advertised for
similar purposes gradually grows less.
BRITONS IN NY ASS ALAND
SOUTH-EAST AFRICA.
Livingstonia, Lake Nyassa, East Central Africa,
Karonga,
26th Feb., 1889.
Gentlemen, —At the urgent request of a friend
in Africa, I have been persuaded to write you
regarding one of the Waterbury Watches, which
I wear every day, and can recommend for time-
keeping ability. It has been in Africa for three
years, travelled from London to Zanzibar, from
Zanzibar to Lake Tanganyika, from Lake Tan-
ganyika to Lake Nyassa. It has been twice
underwater in African rivers, had at least two
falls, and been severely handled by many natives,
many of whom look upon it as something marvel-
ous. Once it was taken down when the spring
leaped from the case, giving us all a fright, and
lay some yards off, a giant thing of many feet in
length. In the fights we have had at Nyassa with
the Arabs, it has been with me in several engage-
ments. Notwithstanding all it keeps good time,
and it is not rivalled by valuable watches. For
wear and tear and time-keeping in a rough country
like Africa, I think there is nothing better.
Believe me, gentlemen, most respectfully yours,
David Kewe Cross, M.8.C.M.,
Ordained Missionary, Livingstonia.
The Waterbury Watch Co.
D.K.C.
F.S.—The watch is one of your humblest.

Noble-street, Newton, Geelong,
10th December, 1888,
To H. F. KuoelmanX, Esq.
Dear Sir,—This is to, certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago w ith a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. I consulted
more than one doctor, and during that time took a great
quantity of v\edieine without it doing me the slightest
good, but I became worse everyday, w&Ming'aivhy whtil
I became a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left lung, a,nd the quantity of bad matter I
was expectorating every day, I became very much alarmed, j
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. I j
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
end of three months I was taken bad one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty s
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in ti e
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1888.
Peter Smythe, J.P.

THE BULLETIN (Published at Sydney, N.S.W)
The Unique Weekly, Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
The Bulletin, the Premier Australian Literary
Journal, is the only paper which has a con*
Bistent and distinctive Australian National
Policy.
THE Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
Payment of Members.
One Man, one Vote.
State Revenue derived directly from the Land.
Complete Secularisation of State Education.
Reform of the Criminal Cods and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
The Bulletin denounces—
Religious Interference in Politics.
Foreign Titles.
The Chinese,
Imperial Federation.
The public eye rejects as uninteresting mors
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the other half that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started seven years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become a vast property, because it pos-
sessed a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
lave unavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have, during the
last year, spent some thousands of pounds in
fitting their buildings with the latest appliances
for high-class printing and the production of illus-
trations, and they have now issued The Bulletin
in an entirely new form—printed with new type
of improved legibility on the finest paper, and
adorned with sketches from the hands of the
ablest and best-paid newspaper artists Australia
has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The voice of The Bulletin is the beacon-fire
of national progress: it is the forceful summons to
the crusade against the monopolies in land,
wealth, power, and privilege—the direct cause of
half the misery which now afllicts British
humanity. Against the claims of avaricious
clergy who claim to monopolise salvation, against
the dark despotism of grasping plutocrats, and
against the exercise of any human authority
which has not been granted by the people over
whom it is exercised, The Bulletin will fearlessly
and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any pther paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the New Zealand
Ranges. A paper which is at once the
most popular city publication and the organ of the
ntelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
The circulation of The Bulletin in Sydney is
unrivalled by that of any other weekly journal,
and more than a hundred copies of it are sold in
Melbourne to one of any other paper printed out-
side that city. Perhaps the strongest proof of The
Bulletin’s popularity is found in the unchallenge-
able fact —easily ascertainable from any postal
official —that, of all journals issued in^Australasia,
The Bulletin is the one most frequently re-
mailed from one reader to another.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
ind best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
irtistic, and its policy the boldest and most
itraightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

Dear Bulletin, — In a recent issue you
write of the funds of the various Australian
labour unions not being able to stand a long drain,
and also that the capitalists could easily put by a
fund of £lOO,OOO. That is a large sum of money,
but it is not equal to one half the amount that is
at the command of the Sydney, Balmain or Red-
fern branches of the Amalgamated Engineers.
The amount of money at the disposal of any one
of these branches is £209,779 135., this being the
amount in reserve funds at the end of 1889. By this
date this sum has increased to about £230,000. The
fundamental principle of this society is that should
any dispute arise between any members and their
employers, the whole of the funds is at the disposal
of the branch affected. Thus at Sunderland the
engineers spent nearly £40,000; and should the
occasion require it, they are quite prepared to
spend a larger amount in Sydney, Melbourne,
Brisbane, Adelaide, or any other part of the world
where its members may have trouble. This society
represents the true federation of labour, having
branches all over the globe, which latter fact ap-
pears to be an objection in the eyes of a very few
in Sydney, as they seem to prefer a purely local
affair. You must not confound the Amalgamated
Engineers with the Marine Engineers’ Association,
or the Australasian Engineers. It takes these
latter societies all their time to support their offi-
cers in affluence, so that they cannot afford to
help others.—Yours, &c.,
AN ENGINEER.
Premier Cockburn was fairly “had” dur-
ing the last few minutes of the recent no-con-
fidence debate. There had been an election for
Yatala the day before, and the Doctor, near the
end of his speech, said that the electors had sup-
ported the policy of the ministry, arid that they
had returned a member who was a ministerial
supporter, and would vote for them. After the
usual moral, the Doctor sat down triumphantly.
Up jumps Butler, the new member. “ After the
speech of the Premier, I have decided to vote
against the Ministry! ” Doctor Cockburn said
nothing, but the look of agony on his face was
most expressive.

Mt Dear Moorabinda, —
The ordinary, novel-reading female enters a local
painter’s atelier with her eye in the corner to see the
bold, bad model dodge behind the crimson curtain. And
she is invariably disappointed. In the Sydney studio
there is the correct air of Bohemianisra and general
scrimmage; brushes, paints, properties muddled all over
the room, but the model—never, no matter when she
calls. Somebody asked me once if the Sydney artists
have lady-models, and I counter-questioned what they
wanted ’em for? In the studio of Percy Spence and
bister Lister, for instance, there are several finished pic-
tures for the N.S.W. Art Society’s show on the 6th—
eleven from the camel’s-hair of Mr. Lister and five from
that of Spence, not one of which calls for the bended arm
and bare shoulders of the lady-sitter. Lister’s are all
landscape—the chief being a large “View on the Lane
Cove River,” valued at a hundred guineas—a deliciously
sweet, cool, convincing picture, without a trace of a
woman in it. Percy Spence’s.great effort is the “Com-
rade’s Grave ” (for the figure in which H. B. Jewett was at
first sketched in) —a horse, a man, and a rude, unkempt
heap of stones in the yellow light of sunset.
The St. John’s Eve cantata given by Sydney University
Musical Society in the great hall on 18th inst. drew the
usual erudite throng which patronises its own talent to
a praiseworthy extent. The performance, owing to in-
adequate rehearsal, wasn’t soothing, but the conductor’s
strategic pause—ostensibly to wither where they stood a
few harmless individuals stealing to catch a tram—saved
the final chorus from a distressing break-down. All the
principal vocalists had horrid colds, and the gown-
ing—except Mrs. Winblad’s white silk and jet, and Miss
Naylor’s pink silk and Maltese lace—made me sorry Eve
ever awoke to the necessity of petticoats.
It’s a terrible truth that you haven’t to scratch Sydney
culture very deep to strike the intellectual Tartar. After
all the gush over “ Doll’s House,” and all the shrill battle-
cries of the Ibsenites, only a nutshell audience ranged
into the School of Arts, on 22nd inst., to hear the gifted
Charrington discourse on the Norwegian ladies’ man with
the aid of a music-stand, a drawing-room set and a tall
black hat. “Brandt,” “Peter Gynt,” and “Pillars of
Society ” were the dramas enthused over to a bored crowd
who punctuated pathos with winter-coughs and kept one
eye on the door where a small boy kept oscillating with a
loud clicking sound, just for the fun of the thing. And
when Mr. C. said he was afraid to touch on “Ghosts” (a
drama treating boldly the delicate question of heredity)
lots of women with bonbonnibres on their knees,
thought he alluded to the supernatural, not social
bogeys, and tittered encouragingly at the “ funny ideas.”
Most of the time was taken up with “Brandt”—a re-
ligious crank run by a demented female who turns up
with straw in her hair at every crisis, and makes nasty
remarks which annoy the hero and cause him to feel em-
barrassed, but there are some very powerful and pathetic
points about Mr. Brandt to which the lecturer did full
justice. Peter Gynt and “Pillars” were only touched
upon, and the worry finished up with a few self-apologies
from Miss Achurch’s husband after which the audience—
including Miss Walenn and Captain Helbert, Judge and
Mrs. Windeyer, Consul Griffin, Mrs. Manning and Mrs.
Charrington bore down the dark passages to the light of
day and the sound of the dinner-gong.
After all, Sydney culture finds it more imposing to air
its superior convictions, per “ letter to the paper,” at a
penny a line, than to dissipate three shillings to sit in an
atmosphere it really doesn’t appreciate, where the chances
of a large, admiring audience are remote. A free lecture
on the “ Improbability of the Improbable,” or any other
unnecessary analysis of nothing, would be crowded to the
steps, but to ask culchaw to lay out money on or about it*
sham pet fad is a mistake all enthusiasts commit until
they “know the ropes.” High-class reasonings behind
the floats, with every suggestive line and situation left in,
draw the cult like nurse-girls to a wedding, because they
like their pill gilded with music, dresses, thuds and
squeals. But Ibsen, mounted with a roll of MS. and a
tumbler of water—well, it isn’t the same thing, my dear.
Marriage is a more or less disastrous undertaking, and
the temper of the old and tired Benedict is apt to get
badly out of gear, even over a fly in the soup, because he
has a heavy load to bear, and boots generally go up when
the twins learn to walk But though rage on the part of
the father of seventeen children is to be looked for, still
you hardly expect a man fresh from his honeymoon to
feel bitterness against mankind, combined with a wild
desire to wage war on his species, do you ? Yet only the
other day one of the masters at a Sydney State Schoo l
returned from his brief connubial Elysium to his scholastic
rule, and the first thing he did was to “ wallop” every boy
in his class! That is the way most people feel after
honeymoon, I expect, though everybody hasn’t th"
materials so handy to vent their feelings on.
Sydney Owl Club held their first annual ball at
West’s, on 21st inst., when over 200 people put in a festive
appearance right at the top of the programme, which
concluded at 2,30 a.m. There were several prettily*
coloured dresses. Mrs. Tollemache (wife of the President)
■vore heliotrope merv and purple velvet with Medici collar
edged with pearls, skirt relieved with mauve chrysan-
themums ; Mrs. Sheridan maize satin, maize crepe mingled
Artistically with pale-grey and pink brocade ; Mrs. John-
Btone, ciel-blue silk and crSpe and diamonds. The supper
was served cafe fashion.
Have you ever remarked that when the fashionable
woman has pink cheeks her baby invariably has pink
cheeks, too ? “ Just my colour, bless the ’ittle sing,” and
the story of the female who dyed her infant’s hair to suit
her own doctored locks is repeated every day, with varia-
tions. Everybody thinks “Baby has mamma’s bright
colour, so mamma's colour is natural, after all.” Chil-
dren—especially the hapless man-traps of sassiety widows
-—do, indeed, deserve the prayers of the congregation.
The Southern Amateur Theatrical Society put grease-
paint on its speaking visage at the Royal Standard on 21st
inst. and went out for to try a fall with the “legitimate” this
time—Watts-Phillips’ “Lady Camilla’s Husband” being
wrestled with in a frantic way for a cast of several. There
was a large, well-garbed house, including Miss Watts-
Phillips (come to see how pa’s little effort bore up under
the affliction, I suppose) in an egg-coloured frock,
chaperoned by Mrs. Rignold in black and cardinal; but
neither Mayor nor Mrs. Burdekin were present—possibly
because they attended the last one. The wail was excel-
lently staged, the village-set being well done, while the
idea of the anvil sounding faintly from “ behind ” was an
inspiration ; and it only the Lady Camilla hadn’t been
reminiscent of a metamorphosed marionette, and if the
husband had tightened-up his belt another hole, things
would have been simply blissful, since the gipsy of Victor
Cohen was a distinct conception, and the dancing of Miss
Andrews evoked a furious encore and a “bokay.”
Not having a guiding “field state” to tell what colour of
hat the Tinpot Creek warriors had on, your Sappho only
simply grasped the great main fact that Moore Park, at
the cadet review on 23rd inst., was covered with a large
number of boys, attired variously and marching extremely
well, who didn’t seem to have so much colonel to the
square inch as is the custom in maturer regiments, and
whose juvenile officers didn’t fall down with the neatnes 8
and celerity of the full-grown major when his charger
kicks a fly off. Lady Carington presented the colours—
red, white, and blue, and, I think, blue and gold—on
horseback, wearing a hunting-habit of black cloth, glossy
beaver and white gloves, and sat her clipped brown
mare to perfection when the geegee objected to
having the colours thrust into its (ace. Lord
Carington wore the picturesque russet uniform and
cock’s feathers of the Colonel Commanding N.S.W.
Cavalry; brother Rupert masqueraded as a Hussar,
and Captain Helbert was rather reminiscent of
street-music in a tight uniform and a weird, burnished
helmet. Sir Robert and Master Hamilton and Captain
Trench drove to the scene of carnage, and Minister for
Education Carruthers was the observed of all on top of a
big bay steed. In the ticket-enclosure there was a
weird commingling of “ society” public-school teacher and
Member of Parliament (headed by Sir Henry Parkes, seen
for the first time in public since his accident) relieved by
one or two professionals—Alfred Plumpton gesticulating
in a tall hat, and tenor Woodfleld standing with his
mouth open to miss the jar of cannonading, according to
the latest scientific principles. The Misses Manning were
there in black gowns and hats, and two well-known Sydney
belles scuttled about like crabs in strange dark-blue
frocks cut d Id Noah’s Ark.
It’s sincerely to be hoped that no “representative’
person felt ill in Sydney on 20th inst., since it was de-
cidedly Esculapius’s day out, and all the doctors (headed
by Sir Alfred Roberts), in glossy top-hats and wondrous
linen, were actively engaged in cheering languidly while
Lord and Lady Carington opened the Carington Convales-
cent Hospital at Camden—Mr. W. H. Paling’s magnificent
gift to the poor. The day encouraged picnic sensations,
and the drive from the station to the hospital under rows
of flags reminiscent of washing was a delightful two-miles’
spin. The ceremony in inaugurating the “gift”—a
handsome two-storey brick structure in dark-grey trimmed
when I gazed on it with red flags, and sitting on top of a
hill overlooking stretches of beautifully-grassed land—led
to the usual remarks, listened toby a flock of Camdenians
in riding-habits, mostly, and corduroys, who opened eyes,
ears, and mouth as Lord Carington, in a blue tie,
chatted inaudibly on the door-step; after which Lady
Carington opened the main entrance with a gilded
key (golden keys open most front doors when you come
to think of it) and everybody rushed wildly inside.
The internal arrangements are the picture of spotless
cleanliness, and there are three nurses and a matron to
keep them so. Rather unkind “ gifts ” to the hospital are
two spikey palms on either side of the vestibule. They
nearly took out my speaking eye lots of times, and if they
remain as placed the chances of a convalescent coming in
with a wooden leg and going out with a dog and a raised-
print Bible are six to one. The Masonic Cottage Hospital,
opened shortly after the Centennial, by Lord Carrington,
is a comfortable villa with a mirror in the hall hat-rack,
which did a brisk trade while the crush lasted, being the
first glass visible all day. The invariable tree-planting and
a sandwich-lunch crowned the proceedings.
The day being fine, fine clothes resulted. Lady Caring-
ton looked charming in heliotrope poplin and velvet with
gold galon, bonnet en suite, the bouquet presented by the
committee being a fragrant arrangement of English prim-
roses, violets, tuberoses, white hyacinth and ferns—
though, with her usual gentle kindliness, Lady Carington
carried a broomstickish posey presented by the Camden
children instead of the more artistic tribute. Mrs. Burde-
kin’s tasteful gown was in mignonette-green cashmere
with panel and Zouave of a design in green velvet arum
lily leaves, toque in green and tomato-red velvet and lily
leaves, black lace parasol. Mrs. W. H. Paling, a hand-
some black velvet and braided mantle, bonnet in green
velvet and pansies; the Misses Lake (her daughters)
sported very fetching black picture hats, and Sir Robert
Hamilton (Governor of Tasmania) wore grey tweed and a
smile, while a naval gentleman moved the spectator to
sympathetic tears in a salmon-pink tie, tan boots and a
silver chain bangle.
“ Paul Jones ” dies in his boots at Sydney Royal, so to
speak. The last but fifth performance, on 23rd inst., saw
a full house, pioneered by Lord and Lady Carington and
Sir Robert and Lady Hamilton. Lady Carington—who
has just recovered from a twinge of rheumatiz—wore a

lace, the bodice made with Medici collar and lace sleeves,
diamond ornaments, the managerial bouquet in white and
violets; Lady Hamilton, a medium height, straight-
featured woman with really golden hair, wearing a blue-
grey silk trained gown with draped front, opera-cloak of
white braided with gold and edged with swansdown—a
managerial bouquet similar to Lady Carington’s aiding
the toilette. Sir Robert (I wonder if he misses his fishing
excursions to the salmon-ponds yet? On one of these
occasions the whole party came back because they had
forgotten, not the bait, but the corkscrew) wore a tasteful
silver star on his coat the size of a cheeseplate, and Lord
Carington contented himself with parting his locks in the
middle. To put the vice-regal guests at their ease,
Snazelle addressed Aggie Kelton as his “ little Hobart
Trumpeter ” in the first act, at which the house was rent
in twain, of course. By-the-by, the strategic non-com-
mittal nature of the new encore verse in “ Two Mariners
Bold’’should induce the thoughtful study ei all people
addicted to taking sides in a crisis.
A square-headed person writes to a Sydney daily,
landing the 24in. waist of the indigenous school “ missle,”
and sighing over the fact that "in a few years Mother
Grundy will declare they are ‘quite too stout,’ and forth-
with their ‘figures’ will be taken in hand by misguided
(ought he not to say Grundy-guided ?) aunts and mothers
. . . until the natural, well-developed waist is cramped
and crushed until it looks like a wasp’s.” Most of which
is quite true. The only thing is, though, that the
budding school-girl doesn’t yearn after a Straight dia-
phragm if she can help it. Frequentlythe “misguided ”
female relative, who dispatches her darling in an unbusked
condition, is deceitfully circumvented by the darling pro-
ducing a leather belt of minimum inches when she gets
round the corner, and holding her breath while an obliging
chum hauls in. The girl who takes a thick waist through
a boarding-school, and brings it out unreduced at the
other end, has the hardihood to hang on to it through
life, no matter whether an army of aunts badgered her
with stay-boxes. And, conversely, the young lady with
hygienic parents but waspish predilections will reveal a
top-shaped outline, even though the only central support
her mamma accords her is a “ Sanitary” combination and
a tape. But, after all, every small waist you see isn’t
tautened up to indigestion point. There is a true story
of a Sydney woman who had to tie “ a thickness ” round
her diaphragm to give the lie to the rumour that she was
disconnected in the middle—and even 17in. centres are
sometimes a natural inheritance. But they are a gift to
the few—an achievement for the many.
If I were a thief, the times I should choose to prowl
round with the bottle-cart and fruit-barrow of duplicity
would be vice-regal “at home ” days. More than hafi
Sydney, I’m sure, pounced down upon Lady Carington on
21st inst. to revenge themselves for the two wet preceding
receptions, and the crush and hubbub in the “red draw-
ing-room ” made even strong men turn pale and make
tracks for the breeze verandah. As was to be expected
on such a Springiab day there were one or two “ forced ”
garments on view, but they were oruel makeshifts. Lady
Carington’s gown was in shot-fawn-and-pbik silk and fawn-
brown velvet, bonnet in gold lace, coronet of blush roses
and fawn-brown strings ; Lady Hamilton effectively wore
drab cashmere draped over a jupe of white amure, revers
of the armure with collar, cuffs, side revers and edgings
of beaver fur, bonnet in drab velvet and pointy white
wings, white parasol. Miss Walenn dark-blue and feathered
hat. Mrs. Fairfax Ross a nobby tailor-built frock in grey
diagonal braided black with black velvet sleeves, small grey
velvet Alsatian bonnet with grey wings and black jet,
grey gloves and furled en-tout-cas— a very workmanlike
rig-out. Miss Burdett Smith bronze silk with flowered
silk side-panels, bonnet en suite. Mrs. Severn (a daughter
of Sir Alfred Stephen) black silk and pansy toque. A
brown cashmere and silk with brown guipure was topped
by a patriotic little hat of wattle-blossoms. Mrs. Char
rington (Miss Achurch) who came with her husband, wore
a chow mantle and a big black hat and veil, and was in
furious demand. Lady Maklay lightened her mourning
by violets and passementerie, and two stout ladies had
crowned themselves with yellow roses. Miss Myee Oar-
ington was hauled about the grounds in the pink of con-
dition and a white sun-bonnet, Master Hamilton was sulky
in loose boot-laces, and the Hon. Rupert Carington with
a straw in his mouth suggested sherry-cobblers, or a
training-track.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

21, was a huge affair, attended by over 1000 people.

a low-necked dress at the theatre last night, and forgot
to take the porous plaster off her back, attracted a good

Lord Carington, accompanied by Sir Robert Hamilton,
was present for a time at the Public-school Sperts, on 21st
inst., on the Association Ground.
Sir Robert Hamilton goes on from Sydney to Brisbane,
and while he is absent Lady Hamilton proposes paying a
visit to Mrs. Lamb, of Rooty Hill. After which their
Hobart vice-regalities go over to Melbourne en route for
the land of raspberries.
Mr. and Lady Constance Combe, at present sojourning
with the Caringtons. flit like bees from one Government
House to another. They will summer in the Cabbage
Garden, and shake our dust off their gaiters for England
next March.
Sir Alfred Stephen, Lieutenant-Governor of N.S.W.,
attained his 88th birthday on 20th August. A congratu-
latory letter from Government House was among the
shower of cards and telegrams received on the auspicious
occasion.
The Australian Club ball-committee anticipate being
worried not a little through the Stewards and Cooks’
Union being on strike. But while a civil-servant remains
with a false beard and a dress-suit the “Awstwalian”
needn’t fear for the success of their race-week festivity, so
far as waiting goes. In which case the guests are advised
to keep a weather eye on the soup and their front
breadths.
Japanese back-scratchers have been introduced into
American society.
The Baroness Clara Dekler, of Tiflis (Roumania) has
invited all the most beautiful women in the world to send
her their photographs, in order to form an album of
beauties during the last ten years of the present century.
A jury of painters and sculptors will judge which of the
photos, are worthy to be placed in the album, which will
be sent to Moscow and preserved in the museum of the
Ethnological Society of that city.
A pretty wedding was that of Miss Augusta Lucy Barber
and the veteran Surgeon Wright, of Sydney, at St. John’s,
Darlinghurst, on 19th inst. The bridal attire was white
corded silk and Honiton lace, and the six bridesmaids
were gowned in the übiquitous buttercup. Mrs. Barber
wore copper-green relieved with pale-blue forget-me-nots
on the bodice. The bride’s going-away gown was in black
and pottery red. There were nearly two hundred guests.

Tin? Bulletin office by Tuesday,
It is with much regret that we draw atten-
tion to the fact that our friend, the poet Farrell,
has, since his accession to the editorship of Sydney
D.T., mounted that most revolting symbol of
bourgeois respectability—a belltopper. Brother
Courtney, we notice, has now also a belltopper.

mining warden’s office, concerning the winding-up

menced, 8th September, 1887 ; amount received by

Northern Miner.
It is an interesting: example of the vigour
of British enterprise that it has spread even so far
as Siberia. Many of the mines in that frozen land
are leased now by British capitalists, and the
Kussian exile, who grinds away his life in un-
speakable wretchedness down in the bowels of the
earth because he was suspected pf wanting a Par-
liament, or a system of local self-government, or
something of the sort, is labouring lo bring in divi-
dends for Christian speculators in London. Also his
wife and his daughter, who were sent along with
him because they were supposed to h»v« evil
thoughts about the Czar, are toiling as b«»ets of
burden for thp benefit of a British shindykit with
an affable smile and a big corporation and a soul
filled with benevolence towards all mankind.
British capital is a ubiquitous thing.
Verse We Have Read.
The Card-Dealer.
Could you not drink her gaze like wine ?
Yet though its splendour swoon
Into the silence languidly
As a tune into a tune,
Those eyes unravel the coiled night
And know the stars at noon.
The gold that’s heaped beside her hand,
In truth rich prize it were ;
And rich the dreams that wreathe her brows
With magic stillness there;
And he were rich who should unwind
That woven golden hair.
Around her where she sits, the dance
Now breathes its eager heat;
And not more lightly or more true
Fall there the dancers’ feet
Than fall her cards on the bright board
As ’twere a heart that beat.
Her fingers let them softly through,
Smooth polished silent things;
And each one as it falls reflects
In swift light-shadowings,
Blood-red and purple, green and blue,
The great eyes of her rings.
Whom plays she with ? With thee who lov’st
Those gems upon her hand;
With me, who search her secret brows ;
With all men, bless’d or bann’d.
We play together, she and we,
Within a vain strange land,
A land without any order, —
Day even as night, (one saith,) —
Where who lieth down ariseth not
Nor the sleeper awakeneth ;
A land of darkness as darkness itself
And of the shadow of death.
What be her cards, you ask ? Even these ;
The heart, that doth but crave
More, having fed; the diamond,
Skilled to make base seem brave ;
The club, for smiting in the dark;
The spade, to dig a grave.
And do you ask what game she plays ?
With me ’tis lost or won ;
With thee it is playing still; with him
It is not well begun;
But ’tis a game she plays with all
Beneath the sway o’ the sun.
Thou seest the card that falls,—she knows
The card that followeth;
Her game in thy tongue is called Life,
As ebbs thy daily breath :
When she shall speak, thou’lt learn her tongue
And know she calls it Death.
DANTE GABRIEL ROSSETTI.
Ten Years Ago.
Dost thou remember the old garden, where
We used to steal,
To build our silly castles in the air,
My pale Lucille ?
I was thy Knight; and thou, my love and Queen,
No shame didst know-
For had we not played—babies on the green—
Ten years ago ?
We part. We meet. Thou statelier grown, and
cold,
X gaunt and grey,
For thou art rich, and I—in sorrows old
Since childhood’s day.
“ Lucille !at last, my love!” Your pale cheek
flames.
“ Did you not know
My husband, sir; we met when was it,
J ames ? ”
“ Ten years ago !”
Well—mine the fault was if I did not please.
You judged the best.
You feared for poverty, and longed for ease,
Comfort, and rest.
His horses stepped as high, your diamonds made
As brave a show,
For all he won them in the tallow trade
Ten years ago.
Yet that white brow, methinks, is less serene
Than in that time,
When bright birds sang and trees and fields were
green
In youth’s fair prime;
When all the world smiled rosy at our feet
In fancy’s glow—
Ah, me! what wondrous dreams we dreamt, my
sweet,
Ten years ago!
Now you are sadly learned, I am told
Five tongues you speak,
You sing, compose—what leaf is that you fold ?
Plato in Greek!
I see —you study at all times—you fret
At progress slow;
You had not needed Greek, dear, had we met
Ten years ago!
Nay, never blush, Lucille. lam not base
To him or you.
From thy soul’s cell no love must his displace
Thy whole life through.
His safeguard and thy solace Ties in this—
Is it not so ?
His constant kindness since the bridal kiss
Ten years ago.
We meet. We part. If life’s bright best be lost,
Much still remains;
Perhaps a higher heaven for him, the cost
Paid with thy pains.
Good-bye, my dear, and if this tale you tell,
These verses show;
Say only, “ This man fought a hard fight well
Ten years ago.”
And ever fights! For if, as churchmen say,
In skies above
Soul mates with soul, as ray melts into ray,
And Heaven is Love,
He will be there, and—if he still loves thee —
Must never know
That thou on earth hadst e’er a thought for me,
Ten years ago.

The Sydney Clerical Divorce.
The sorely-afflicted better-half of the Rev.
Andrew Gardiner, of Sydney, has got a rule nisi
for a divorce against her more or less wicked
husband, who is understood to be now lecturing on
morality and things to the good young men of the
holy city of San Francisco. After reading in
the daily press the evidence in the affair.
Which, summed up, appears only to amount
to a tolerably strong 1 case of suspicion, We are
somewhat at a loss to know the exact grounds. Ob
which the lady has succeeded in getting rid of her
burden. If the law, as laid doWn by the learned
Judge in favour of Mrs. Gardiner, is to stand a&
the law of the land, it may be as well, considering
the already mixed condition of the Australasian
matrimonial laws, to glance at the condition of the
tI.S. marriage laws, as laid down by a reputable
Californian paper:—
Marriage and divorce are regulated in America
by State laws, and in consequence there are as
Many different laws as there are States. These
different State laws vary according to the moral
Strictness or laxity of the people. A civil ceremony
is valid in all the States, except Maryland ; tlifj
Court of Appeals has decided that in that State it
is necessary td have “a religiods service of some
kind in Order to constitute a Valid marriage;”
.Kentucky declares, bjv its Statute, tMt “Marriage
is prohibited arid declared void when hot solem-
nized Or contracted in the presence of ail
authorised person Or society.” Those in Mary-
land, however, who do not desire a religious
ceremony, may go in an hour to Washington City,
where another and different Marriage laW exists.
Those in Kentucky who may object to any cere-
mony can cross the Ohio River and find in Ohio,
Indiana Or Illinois a Marriage law lax enough to
satisfy the most liberal-minded free-lover. In
North Carolina the later decisions are so
ambiguous that the question as to what
really constitutes marriage is left in doubt.
Tennessee requires a ceremony for whites, but not
for negro marriages. The United States District
Court held that a ceremony was necessary to mar-
riage in California and Oregon; but as this de-
cision lias been overruled in California, it may not
be good law in Oregon. In Delaware, Maine,
New Hampshire, and Vermont the question is
left in doubt. It is an open question, never
having been directly decided upon, in Arkansas,
Colorado, Connecticut, Dakota, Florida, Idaho,
Montana, Nebraska, Nevada, New Mexico, Vir-
ginia, and Washington. There is a large number
of States where it is well settled that no ceremony
at all is necessary. In other words, people may
marry themselves. They need no officiant or wit-
nesses. Over thirty millions of American citizens
live under taws where the mere interchange of
consent to become man and wife may constitute
legal marriage. Mr. Bishop, in his work on
■ * Marriage and Divorce” (vol. 1, p. 184), says :
The leading principle is, that consent makes
marriage. No form or ceremony, civil or religious,
no consummation or cohabitation, no writing, no
witnesses even, are essential.” In New York, and
other States adopting the same policy, mutual con-
sent is all that is necessary to constitute a binding
marriage contract. In California agirl of 13 declined
at first a proposal of marriage on account of her
youth, but accepted later No ceremony, civil or
religious, was performed; it was not shown that
Mey both exchanged consent. But they were
■egally married. In most States, a man and
woman who agree to and do live together as
tusband and wife, and introduce each other to
the world as such, are legally married. Thus we
find a confusing variety of marriage laws, as
among the different States. In some, a civil
ceremony is required; in others, both a civil and
religious ceremony is necessary ; and in still
others, a marriage may be valid with no ceremony
at all. That which would constitute a legal
marriage in one State would be either concubinage
or bigamy in another. If the home is the founda-
tion of the State, and marriage the foundation of
the home, then thete should be but one marriage
Idtv, uniform and just, throughout all the States
and Territories Of the Union. At present, a boy
df fourteen and a girl of twelve may run away
from school and legally marry without the consent
or knowledge of their parents. In fact, the line of
prohibition is only drawn clear and sharp at seven
years. This is an outrage upon common decency.
Such is the present conflict of laws, as applied to
divorces, that a man may be divorced in one State
and still married in another. In one State he may
marry agan, in another he becomes a criminal if
he does. We frequently hear in this day of
“ divorces which are not valid extra-territorially;”
“ A New York man married an Ohio girl; they
liVed together in .New York ; had trouble ; she re-
turned to her pareiits in Ohio, and after some
years brought suit, arid a decree of absolute
divorce was granted her, the only service being by
publication in a Ideal Ohio paper. When he
heard of this ho married a New York woman;
and was promptly artested, and found guilty
of bigamy.” So the divorce that was valid
in the State of Ohio, was worthless in New
York. In Ohio they were both single and able
to marry ; in New York they were still married to
each other. ■ His children in New York by another
woman would be illegitimate, and have no pro-
perty rights. The divorced wife in Ohio would
continue to have a wife’s interest and title in all
his property in New York. Thus by this conflict
of laws he is a husband without a wife and she a
wife without a husband. Among the various legal
causes for divorce are the following: “Insanity”
before marriage in most of the States, and insanity
after marriage is a legal ground for divorce in two
States. “ Imprisonment ” prevails as a cause in 15
States, and mere “ conviction of crime ” in over 30.
The term of the imprisonment varies from “life,”
as in Connecticut, down to “ one year,” as in New
Hampshire, and in other States it appears to bo
immaterial. In still other States it is only neces
sary to be guilty of “ gross misbehavior,”
as in Rhode Island. In Missouri, “ vagrancy ”
is sufficient ground, and her legal defini-
tion of a vagrant is : “Every person who may
be found loitering around houses of ill-fame,
gambling-houses, or places where liquors are sold
or drunk, without visible means of support, or
shall attend or operate any gambling device or
apparatus, or be engaged in practising any trick
or device to procure money or other thing of value,
or shall be engaged in any unlawful calling what-
ever.” Is it possible to conceive of a law more
open to abuse than this? “Unehastity” before
marriage, concealed from the other party, is suffi-
cient to render the union null and void in twelve
States. In three States —Kentucky, Massa-
chusetts, and New Hampshire—divorces have
been granted on the ground of certain changes in
religious opinions. Then there is the follow-
ing group of causes, which embrace all
the easy divorce States Cruelty,” “ vio-
lence to person,” “indignities,” “severity,”
“great bodily injury or great mental distress,”
“gross misbehaviour or wickedness,” “gross
neglect of duty,” “public defamation,” “endan-
gering the reason of the other,” “ habitual indui*
gence of violent and ungovernable _ temper,'’
“desertion” for, from “five years” in Rhode
Island, to “ one year” in California, and “habitual
drunkenness ” for, from “ three years ” in a num-
ber of States, to “one year” in Missouri and
California. Then, to cap the climax, in some
States the list of causes for divorce is ended by
“any other cause within the discretion of the
court.” This reduces marriage to the low level of
concubinage, at the pleasure of, the parties and the
option of the courts ! Reading all this, one can
easily understand that the divorce granted by
some Yankee authority to the alleged bigamist
M‘Leod is genuine enough.
To Travellers.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
‘i All ire give to travellers is a pannikin of flour.”
Did you ever take “the wallaby” along some
dreary track
With that hideous malformation, called a swag,
upon your back ?
Did you ever seek a billet dn the squatters’ stamp-
ing-ground
Where wide bare plains are ’neath you and the
burning suit all round ?
Did you ever ask for rations in the dusky gloam-
ing hour,
And humbly from the squatter take “a pannikin
of flour ? ’
Then the meanness of both parties to this paltry
little play
Must have struck you : “What shall end it?” is
the question of to-day.
We all know about the manner in which station
cheques are spent;
Have a notion that the squatter views the boozer
with content.
Guess he likes to see his workmen come along
depressed and poor,
Dor the man without a copper is the rich man’s
victim sure.
There are fifty-thousand “whalers” without a foot
of land,
There are twice five-hundred squatters hold Aus-
tralia in their hand.
Did you ever hear of Jacob? how he played that
little game
With his pottage and the birthright of Bub Esau?
That’s the same.
Well, ’tis time the people said it—all such beggary
must cease
In this mighty home of Ereedom and of limitless
increase :
Let us frame our land-enactments so that right
shall hold the sway,
While the Land is for the People, and the Land-
lords fade away.

A Melbourne City Councillor has given
himself up for embezzlement, and one bolted some
years ago, but there is scarce another black sheep
on the records, which are thus exceptionally clean.
In New York' nineteen aldermen scuttled over
their complicity in old Adaln Sharp’s railway
frauds—his bribing to get the cable-trams,along
Broadway.
How is it that almost every member of
Parliament, public orator and newspaper cor-
respondent talks glibly about “ creating a
Frankenstein?” Nearly everybody is comparing
the strike to “a Frankenstein,” instead of to a
Frankenstein’s monster. The noble public should
take another turn through Mrs. Shelley’s book.
Dear Bulletin,— All persons acquainted
with Esmarch’s popular or scientific works, must
have been greatly surprised when they read in last
week’s Bulletin that this great surgeon considers
water filtered through a cemetery harmless and a
cheap beef-tea. Your informant evidently en-
tirely misunderstood Esmarch’s sarcastioal way of
speaking. No surgeon can abhor the remotest
contact with dead bodies more than Esmarch does;
at all his graver operations spectators are only ad-
mitted on their solemn word of honour that they
have not touched a dead body or been present at a
post-mortem during the previous fortnight; and,
great as the interest is which he takes in the p.m.
examinations of the few patients who die under
his care, he can never lie persuaded to be per-
sonally present, but receives the report of his
special assistant for this purpose (the “ septic
assistant” as he is called) in the open and dis-
infecting sea-breeze of the Baltic shore. Much
against his will Esmarch once presented me with
a surgical instrument and a biting lesson. When
coming from anatomy class as a medical student
in Kiel, I met Esmarch, who, while passing me,
dropped a small instrument from a pouch ho
carried. I quickly picked it up for him, but to
my intense annoyance the great surgeon refused
to accept it again, and keeping carefully several
yards away from my infected presence, he begged
me in his most cutting style to remember, for my.
future life, that an instrument once touched by a
person just come out a dead-house was unclean
and unfit for surgical use for ever afterwards.—
Rejected.

* * *
The waiting group included Superinten-
dent Darrell, Dr. Grocott, and Chaplains Taylor
and M‘lndoe. These names are placed in the
order of their official importance. Compared with
Superintendent, a doctor ,and a clergyman were,
in the estimation of the System, particularly siriall
potatoes indeed.
The Superintendent having bowed to the ladies,
called the .Commandant aside. ,
~ “ Taylor’s going to retake a litiie row, I think,
sir. He’s goirig to ask the ladies pot to go in the
truck if that,fellow Tappiri—the forger, you know
—is kept in the team.”
“D—-n his iiripucjerice !” blustered the C.C,
“If lie interferes with discipline, I’ll suspend
him.”
“I, think I would, sir, if I were you, That
Tappin’s becoming more desperate everC day!
Why, he assaulted the Chaplain himself last
flight ”
, “He did, did lie? Oh, and I suppose the
parson is coining the‘turning, o’, t’other cheek’
dodge—expects to irielt tile, obdurate heart by
hiagnariimioy arid sort of rot, does he?
sneered the Com ni arid ait t'. “I’ll show him the
System knows a little better how to deal with the
Tappin tribe than the Church does.”
; The two admirable administrators advanced to
join the others, and came up to them in time to
hear the Protestant Chaplain remark —
“You must know, Mrs. Browning, there is
what is called ‘ chummage.’ Everyone who comes,
bond or free, has to pay his or her ,‘ footing.’ He
or she must do something for his or her circle.”
The parson spoke with an affected jocularity, so
as to diminish the serious aspect of this attempt
to foil the authorities.
“Yes?” laughed Mrs. Browning. “ And must
I pay my ‘ chummage,’ as you call it?”
“We can make no exception,” responsively
smiled Mr. Taylor. Now, to pay your footing,
will you do me a favour?”
“ If I can—and will be thankful if you will let
me off so lightly.”
“Then, dear madam, pray ask the Commandant
to remove Convict Tappin from D Gang !” In a
few rapid sentences he sketched for the lady
Tappin’s first and present condition—how, though
a convict, he was, from education and training,
acutely sensible to his degraded surroundings,
and how the man’s spirit recoiled violently from
membership in the worst gang on the Island.
Mrs. Browning, troubled to the shallows of her
butterfly nature more by Mr. Taylor’s earnestness
of tone than by his narrative, looked round for
her husband, that he might advise her, but the
lieutenant had strolled to the edge of the quarry
and was looking down at the workers. Then she
looked at the Commandant. He took the glance
for a question, and was about to reply, when
Darrell, fearing that the Chaplain would obtain
his wish, interrupted. Darrell knew the Com-
mandant ; knew that a woman’s request would have
more weight with him than a volume of soundly-
reasoned representations from an official.
“Permit me a word on the Chaplain’s state-
ment, Mrs. Browning. Mr. Taylor speaks from
the standpoint of the Church, you know, and
looks to the individual. We have to regard the
System and the mass. Leniency to this man
would foment a revolt among all those in D Gang,
who have not committed criiries so awful as his.”
“ Less awful than his, Mr. Darrell!” ejaculated
the Chaplain, astonished. “You know this man
is a forger—nothing more !”
“You surely forget, Mr. Taylor, that Tappin
committed sacrilege. Did he not outrage the
sanctity of the Church in assaulting you, sir? We
must punish him for that, if for nothing else,”
said the Superintendent, with an overflowing sar-
casm in his voice.
The Chaplain winced at the jibe, but all he said
was that “ Tappin was not sent toD. Gang for the
offence of striking himself, which offence had not
been reported.”
“ Do you mean to say, Mr. Darrell,’’asked Mrs.
Browning, horrified, “ that this prisoner really
assaulted' Mr. Taylor ?”
“ Yes,” replied Darrell. “But Mr. Taylor is
so generous as to overlook it. But we, who are
responsible for discipline, can’t afford to go by
the Sermon on the Mount. We’ve got to obey the
Queen’s Regulations. And there’s a slight differ-
ence between the two !”
“Oif the man is so degraded as to strike a
clergyman,” exclaimed Mrs. Browning, who had
all her sex’s superficial reverence for “the cloth,”
“ I couldn’t think of interfering.”
“That’s right,” said the Commandmant. “I
shouldn’t have liked to refuse your first re-
quest, Mrs. Browning ; and yet it would have been
awkward to have granted this.”
“But, my dear madam, perhaps I didn’t make
myself clear,” impetuously cried the Chaplain. ,
‘ Perhaps you don’t understand that this man, as
one of D. Gang, will have to pull you up the hill
an these trucks to-day. The other men are accus-
tomed to the work; they are indeed beasts —I
won’t say whose fault it is that they are so ; they
can’t be degraded lower, but this man—surely you,
an English lady, will not consent to be drawn by
a man who, abased as he is, is, or has been, an
English gentleman ! ”
“An English gentleman ! Well, that is good !”
laughed the Commandant uproariously. “ Why,
Chaplain, you’re a perfect humourist! An Eng-
lish gentleman—ah ! ah !”
“ I’m sure all English gentlemen will feel
honoured at your—ah—comparison of them to a—
doubly-convicted forger,” said Dr. Grocott.
“Oh, Mr. Taylor,” said Mrs. Browning, “if
the System and Justice requires the man’s punish-
ment, and puts him into the team, I don’t see that
a lady can object.”
Cautiously Mr. Taylor then intimated that
neither he nor his wife could join the excursion.
Father MTndoe said that he sympathised with his
reverend brother’s position in the matter, and
would also, if the Commandant would allow him,
withdraw his acceptance of the latter’s kind invi-
tation.
“ You can do just as you please,” shortly said
the great man.
# # *
The constraint of this just-recorded inci-
dent soon evaporated and the picnieers took their
seats in the trucks, making the process one of
much merriment. Most of the ladies, the children,
and the Commandant and Doctor Grocott were in
the foremost waggon, and the rest of the party
gathered in the other.
When they were all seated, Overseer Crunch
came to the Commandant for instructions.
“Are we to go slow or quick, sir?” he asked,
touching his cap.
The Commandant turned to his guests. “-Well,
ladies, what do you say ? Shall we go slowly or
quickly? You command here to-day, you know,”
was his gallant remark.
The ladies tittered, but none spoke. Then—
“ Which is hardest on the poor men?” asked
Mrs. Browning’s child Nellie. Nellie had not for-
gotten Martin’s death.*
“Oh, don’t think of that at all,” rejoined
Scragge.
“ But which would be ?” the girl continued, with
a child’s persistence.
What the Commandant would have answered
it is impossible to say. Little Ada Budgeori
made a laughable interjection—at least so it
seemed to Messrs. Scragge and Crunch.
■ Turning to her playmate) Nellie, she exclaimed ;
“You silly thing! You called the prisoners
poor men ! Convicts airi’t men—ate they, ML
Scragge ?”,
. Unlike Nellie)_ tliis child had beeii born find bred
in the “Establishment;” She hail lived iri trie
atmosphere of a corivict-statiqri froth birth, and iri
the depths of her infantile experience she had dis-
covered the profound truth Underlying the System
that “ convicts airi’t men.”
Her remark became the Island hon mot.
. ..When his laughter subsided the Commandant
said to Crunch : “ We’re ready. By the way,
where have you put Tappin ?”
“His Washup, sir?” grinned the blackguard.
‘ I’ve made him unicorn, by Mr. Darrell’s orders,
sir. He’ll come better under the whip, so.”
“Very well,” said the Commandant.
* * #
“Ready, all!” shouted Overseer Crunch.
Overseer Crunch’s driver—Convict Cracknecke —
took his tall whip and clasped its butt lovingly ;
Overseer Crunch’s “animals” drew up to their
chains.
Twenty couples and one “ unicorn leader” made
up the team. Through the double rank of the
‘‘ animals” ran a light but strong chain. At foot-
intervals along the chain, to a link of doubled
thickness, were attached two swivels, one on either
side, and to every swivel a rope, three feet in
length. livery man of the couples clasped a rope.
The “ unicorn ’man—Tappin, English ex-mayor,
forger, devil—-had even less “ purchase” on the
chain than his comrades. His hands were free,
but a rope passed round his waist, and was fastened
to the end of the chain. He had to perform his
part of the work of haulage by the waist only.
The peculiar advantage of being “ unicorn” (in
the official judgment) was this—that while the
other men had occasionally a chance of “shirking
their puli” without detection, the wretch har-
nessed as solitary “leader” could not relax his
exertions for a second without being found out,
for the slackening of his energies would at once be
apparent in the slackening of his rope.
This was Tappin’s second day in the team, and
his first as unicorn. The team had already made
two journeys up the hill this morning, once with
the eatables and table accessories for the picnic,
and the other with the assistants of Mr. Knatch-
bull and that worthy himself, who was to super-
vise the arrangements at the Dell. On each of
the former trips Convict Tappin had been com-
pelled by sheer weakness to slacken his rope ; but
the vigilance of Overseer Crunch had instantly
perceived this manifestation of incapacity, and
corrected it. Anything like shirking called for a
delicate attention on the part of Driver Crack-
necke.
“Ready, all!” repeated Crunch. Cracknecke,
who had stationed himself opposite the middle
couple, flung out his long lash .
Mrs. Browning almost wished she had not come.
It was really unpleasant to hear a cry of pain,
even though it was only from an “animal.”
* * *
Dp the steep hillside crept the team. It
was going “ slow,” for which the “animals ” were
as thankful as brutes could be, for to go quickly
chafed their ankles the more. A slow pace meant
that at least twenty steps could be taken without
a fresh bit of skin being worn off by the basil;
while a quick step would have fretted open a new
wound every ten paces.
The slow pace gave Mrs. Browning an oppor-
tunity of viewing the noble panorama that spread
itself before her cultivated eye as the waggons
mounted the eminence. Her qualmishness passed
away, lost in admiration. First she expressed her
gratitude to Dr. Grocott for having suggested the
drive to her, and then :
“What an exquisite scene!” she said to Mr.
Scragge. “ How harmonious the blending of the
colours ! Even the yellow uniform of the prisoners
does not seeiri out of place!” The Commandant
agreed.
Mrs. Budgeonhad been making observations of
a different sort.
“ How funny, isn’t it, Doctor,” she remarked to
Grocott, “to watch these men in harness. Like a
huge centipede, aren’t they ?”
“ Only,” laughed the surgeon, “they don’t own
a hundred feet. They have only eighty-two legs
between ’em—and one of those is wooden.”
So they jested—while the team breasted the hill,
under the whip of Driver Cracknecke, and in due
time landed the pic-nicers on the crest of the
ridge. Arrived at the Dell, the pleasure-makers
set about the business of enjoyment without loss
of time.
* * *
At lunch the ladies were profuse in superla-
tives; and even the military merf found it not
inconsistent with their soldierly dignity to express
praise of the spot and the perfection of the
arrangements.
“Ah, demin’d good style Scragge has done this
thing in, hasn’t he ? ” said the Major to Lieu-
tenant Budgeon.
“Yes, sir ; but, then, you know (and the Lieu-
tenant went as near to winking as he dare to his
commanding officer) any one could do the same
who had the run of the Storesd’
“But the Government Stores don’t include
wines, and olives, and—and things.”
The Lieutenant, who had been on the “civil
side ” for a year as locums teiicns at Port Arthur,
smiled a smile of superior intelligence, as befitting
a man who had been behind the scenes.
“ Oh, don’t they, sir ! See that ham—a genuine
Westphalian by the look of it. Now, that would
be probably invoiced by the Hobart Town Store-
keeper to the C.C. here as ‘25 basils,’or, 1 doz.
shirts !’ ”
“ God bless my soul! ” said the Major, “But
surely that’s against the Regulations ? ”
“Well,” was the rejoinder, “I suppose it is.
But it’s done at every convict-station for all that,
as a regular thing.”
_ The probability that he was masticating a por-
tion of a shirt, or a fragment of a basil, did not
prevent the Major’s enjoyment of a slice of the
ham with his chicken, a little later.
A couple of hours after the luncheon, Com-
mandant Scragge suggested they should “chase
the glowing hours with flying feet.” What
he meant was, that they should have a dance.
That splendid organiser, Knatchbull, had foreseen
this —had prepared for it. He had a couple of
convict-violinists in attendance, he had had a part
of the grassy carpet scythe-mowed, he had even
frirnished Mr. Scragge with a programme. Capital
master of the ceremoriies, Knatchbull! The sug-
gestion was welcomed with enthusiasm. Only
Mrs. Browning thought the mown plot was not
large enough to accommodate the dancers.
“ That’s easily reifledied,” said the Comman-
dant ; and he instructed Knatchbull to direct one
of the convict servaiits to pass the scythe over a
larger area;
“ But, sir,” said coolly, “ I’ve
returned the scythe to your gardener. ” Herein
Mr. Knatchbull lied. The scythe had riot been
returned to the Settlement; it was at that very
moment secreted behind a clump of bracken, a
hundred yards away, and within ten feet of the
spot where the tramway-team would take up its
load of.picnicers on their return.
So the Commandant and his guests had to go
on with their dance on the turf as prepared by
Mr. Knatchbull. The spot answered very w r ell
after all; and they went through quite a long list
of dances on that most delightful of ball-rooms ;
and the shadows were lengthening and broadening
into an early twilight before the feet ceased to
twinkle on the yielding verdure, and the fiddles to
throb and thrill.
“A most happy day!” sighed Mrs. Browning
to the Commandant. “I’m sure I shall never
forget it. I can never thank you sufficiently.”
“ I’m sufficiently repaid, madam,” replied that
gallant gentleman, “by the fact that you have
enjoyed yourself.” Really, he thought, he must
not forget to give Convict Knatchbull a glass of
wine !
* * *
For what reason did Convict Knatchbull
secrete that scythe ? True to his instincts, he had
used the freedom granted to him so that he might
prepare for the picnic to “botanise.” On this
occasion, however, it would be the Government
which would suffer if his “ plant” were successful,
and not some poor devil of a prisoner. Convict
Knatchbull had, as a fact, plotted an insurrection.
Like all great conceptions, his plan was simpli-
city itself. The picnic, he reflected, would gather
together every man on the island in superior
authority, military and civil. All these men, with
the exception of Superintendent Darrell, would
return by the trucks at the time. Now, if on the
home trip the trucks could be upset or derailed,
their occupants would almost certainly meet w’ith
a sudden end ! In any case, they would be
disabled, and at the mercy of the insurrectionists.
This w r as Convict Knatchbull’s idea. All that
was necessary to its fulfilment was the co-opera-
tion of the D. Gang—the tramway team. On the
down trip, the waggons were lowered by the team,
and not hauled. If, then, at a given signal the
“animals” let go their ropes, the trucks
would dash to destruction. At the first
curve on the line they would certainly leap
the track, and the people in them would be thrown
or carried down the precipitous height. The rest
would be easy. With the chief authorities dead
or wounded, the subordinate officers at Longridge
and the Cascades, taught by the System to be
mutually distrustful, would find it impossible to
combine, and would submit to the rebels, and the
company of soldiers at the Settlement, deprived
of their officers, would make only the briefest
stand against the freed convicts.
Such was Convict Knatchbull’s plan. He had
used the Commandant’s pass to develope the
scheme more than he had used it for the picnic
preparations. He had broached the project to the
recognised leader of D. Gang—Johnson—care-
fully. Johnson had seen its feasibility but
doubted Knatchbull. “’Nother plant-of
yours, Cap’n John?” he sneeringly asked. “Wot
has old Scragge promised yer this time —a free
pardon ?” Knatchbull, in reply, had sWorn by his
Maker that he was going to be true to the men
this time ; and Johnson consented to consult his
fellow-demons.
“ I don’ b’leeveyer, Knatchbull,” he said, “but I
tell yer this, I can lay a plot, too, an’ I’ll take care
if you trap us this time, yer won’t give Crown
evidunse agen us—dead ’uns don’ tell tales, an’
they don’ give evidunse either. I don’t b’leeve
yer, I say, but I likes th’ plan. If it fails, well,
we’ll swing, o’ corse, but yer’ll be stiff afore we
do.” Knatchbull rejoined that he would take the
risk. He could not know that Convict Johnson
chuckled to himself at the reply ; for let the plot
succeed or fail, Johnson’s notion was to wreak
vengeance on Knatchbull for his former treachery.
It it succeeded Knatchbull should be sacrificed;
it it failed, Knatchbull shouldn’t survive to
“ peach.” Knatchbull, on the other hand,
reasoned that if it succeeded, his resourceful
intellect would be indispensable to the rebels;
while, if it failed, he didn’t doubt that his infernal
ingenuity would secure him immunity from both
the wrath of the authorities and the vengeance of
his fellow-prisoners.
D Gang, to a man—including Tappin—fell in
with the suggestion : the more readily as nearly
all of its members had a personal grudge against
Knatchbull.
* * «
Superintendent Darrell paid his adieux to
the Settlement party as soon as the ladies were
seated in the trucks for the homeward journey.
This was what Knatchbull had counted on. That
villain had been left at the picnicing ground to
complete the packing-up and removal of the
picnic etceteras, but had stayed no longer than
was necessary to lay his hands on everything that
would serve as a weapon of attack.
He had followed the pleasure-makers at a few
minutes’ interval and had stationed himself behind
some stunted guava-trees, whence, in a moment,
he could reach the secreted scythe. The particular
purpose for which he designed that useful and
dangerous article was the disablement of Overseer
Crunch and Driver Cracknecke. He had given
himself an hour’s practice with it and he smiled to
himself as he thought how expertly he could
manage that turn of the wrist by which the for-
ward sweep of the scythe (which was to cut down
Crunch) would be merged into the recoil (which
was to leave a vacancy in the drivership). A
splendid organiser, Knatchbull ! He left nothing
to chance. ®
# * «
“Ready, all!” cried Overseer Crunch
“ Forward !” Craoknecke let his fifteen-foot lasti
leap to the front and then curl to the roar—and the
descent began.
Johnson was “off-side” of the couple next th
second truck. He it was who was to give the
signal to “ let go,” and he tvas in such a pdsitiori
that all iri the gang could observe the signal.
D gang had a secret code of communication of
its own. “No. 98,” in this glossary of rascality
was “Do it—now!” “No. 98” itself was ex’
pressed by the right hand being brought sharply
to the “salute,” three times in rapid succession
Just when the waggons were getting way ’ on
them, at a spot where the wooden parallels took a
sudden dip, Johnson signalled and gave the truck
next him a mighty push.
With a yell, the off-side and near-side rows of
the gang flung their rope-ends to the right and to
the left, and, as quickly as they could with their
fettered legs, got off the track and out of the Wav
of the chain. ™
Freed from the restraint of the men’s pull, and
as though they were animate things, the trucks
paused in their motion—rooked to and fro f or a
second—and then, with a jerk and a jump, rushed
down the breathless decline.
* # #
But what is that dragging at the far end
of the chain ? That, a few yards traversed
slackens its tension on the line of links by a sudden
leap forward ? That, in the same movement, pro-
jects itself (so it seems, in the fast-encroaching
darkness) into the brushwood that borders the
track ? That clasps a young pine, and swings itself
round the bending but stalwart trunk? That
shudders and groans as the chain which binds it
to the waggons feels the resistance—and obeys it—
and compels the trucks, in their turn, to slow
down —to pause—to stop ? What is that thing—
crushed, maimed, bloody ? Oh, that’s the carcase
of Convict Tappin—“ Tappin, of the Ring,” who
in this eccentric fashion, has chosen to violate his
oath to the D Gang, and to foil the skilfully-laid
plans of Convict Knatchbull.
The gang, after one dreadful moment of stupefied
surprise, again yelled, and were about to rush
some towards Tappin, others towards the trucks'
when the report of a pistol was heard above the
shouts, and one of the team stumbled and fell on
the bracken, which he clutched and tore in his
death-struggle.
“Back to your ranks, men; back, I say!”
Darrell, apparently cool, certainly collected, strode
down from the ridge-top and waved them back
with his smoking pistol. Only one firm, courageous
man to withstand that fierce on-rush of devils—
and yet they obeyed him ! Some saw behind
him —shapeless, intangible, but still awful—the
Majesty of British Law ; others—and these were
the majority - concluded that Knatchbull had
“sold” them again, and that behind Darrell
behind the stunted guava-trees and young
pines, was ranged the military strength of the
Settlement. Before they could perceive their
error, the officers in the trucks had forced them-
selves from the shrieking women and had jumped
out. They placed themselves by Darrell’s side.
With Crunch and Cracknecke they formed but a
paltry dozen, and no more than two were armed.
But they represented Authority— and the cowed
wretches slunk back to their files.
Johnson alone retained a hope of carrying the
emeute through. He sent a hurried whisper along
the ranks. Darrell—vain and tyrannical though
he was—was brave to the core. He saw one of
the gang bend his head to his neighbour.
Darrell stepped forward, and gripped him.
“ It’s you, Johnson, is it ? I should have thought
you’d had enough of the gag! You shall have
some more—as a preliminary to the hemp.” He
hauled Johnson out and handcuffed him. He
called the gang to “attention,” and then ordered
them forward to the Barracks. They went like
lambs.
The others then thought of Tappin. Somehow
or other the ladies and children had got out of the
trucks, and the empty waggons alone were held
by the chain to which Tappin was fastened, and to
which he himself had constituted a brake. They
released him —more dead than alive. All, even
the frivolous women, in the shook of their terror,
understood what he had done for them. One or
two tried to thank him ; he cursed them ; he hadn’t
done it for them ; they could have gone to h——
for all he cared ; but he couldn’t bear to be an
accessory to thekilling of that tiny lot. He pointed,
as he muttered the last words, to Nellie Browning.
Not for many days after did they learn, however,
why the presence of the child should have res-
trained him from partnership in a hideous deed.
They found then that the girl, when visiting the
quarry, had smiled him her thanks because he
pulled her some spice-plant. The remembrance of
this incident had determined him, at the last
moment, not to free his rope from the chain
# # *
Darrell’s timely appearance on the scene
was accounted for by the yell which the gang had
raised when they first let their ropes fall. There
was an exultant note in the shout which in-
stinctively spoke to him of revolt, and he rushed
back. But for Tappin, however, he would have
been too late.
The same yell explained why Convict-Writer
Knatchbull did not wield the scythe. He had
grasped it, in readiness for the signal, but the
shout had surprised him—had, indeed, alarmed
him. Splendid organiser as he was, he had not
calculated upon it, and he knew that it would
recall Darrell, and his former quarter-deck experi-
ence reminded him how one bold, resolute man in
authority could quell a mutinous crew. So, in-
stead of mowing down Messrs. Crunch and Crack-
necke, he ran quickly back to the picnicing
ground—and finished packing the plate.
As to the consequences of the emeute , are they
not written in the records of the Special Commis-
sion for the year, and in the black register of the
Island graveyard ? Some time or other wo may
steal a page from both.

«JTIHE THREE GREAT EIGHTS!
The Needles Patent Gas-making Machine;
The Harvey Patent Safety Kerosene Lamp;
And the New Era Portable Gas Lamp.
The Harvey Lamp has been adopted by the
Railway Commissioners for lighting the Sydney
trams. We were awarded the Highest Order of
Merit and Special Mention by experts at the
late Melbourne Exhibition for our lighting
mediums. Over 6000 Harvey Lamps are in use
in this colony, and for all lighting purposes are
giving universal satisfaction. These Lamps can
be seen on one of the North Shore ferry-boats,
and are suitable for all classes of vessels. The
Inventor offers £2OO to anyone producing as good
a Kerosene Burner.
Patentees for Australasia:
H. T. Smith Sc Co., 494 Kent St. South, Sydney;
14 Arcade, Adelaide, S.A.; Argent Street, Broken Hill;
Parkside Iron Works, Melbourne.
